Visual Studio 2013 Launch today was the Monaco editor in Visual Studio Online. </p> \n<p>I've seen little hints of this coming - for example, Scott Hanselman's post in August: <a href=\"http://www.hanselman.com/blog/ARichNewJavaScriptCodeEditorSpreadingToSeveralMicrosoftWebSites.aspx\">A rich new JavaScript code editor spreading to several Microsoft web sites</a>. And if you looked around the command-line Kudu interface on an Azure Web Site (available at https://&lt;SITENAME&gt;.scm.azurewebsites.net) there's been a lightweight file editor available for a little while.</p> \n<p>After the announcement today, I dug up enough info to be dangerous and took it for a quick spin. Quick disclaimer: this is me playing around for a bit, it's not an official announcement.</p> \n<h2>Some top Monaco editor links</h2> \n<p><a href=\"http://blogs.msdn.com/b/somasegar/archive/2013/11/13/visual-studio-2013-launch-announcing-visual-studio-online.aspx\">Visual Studio 2013 Launch: Announcing Visual Studio Online</a> - Soma's announcement post gives a quick overview. More importantly, it links to a series of videos on Channel 9 which overview the Monaco editor and demonstrate using it in a few different scenarios.</p> \n<p>Channel 9 series: <a href=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco\">Visual Studio Online \"Monaco\"</a></p> \n<ul> \n <li> <p><a href=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/First-Steps\">First Steps</a> (2 minutes, 26 seconds)</p> </li> \n <li> <p><a href=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/Navigating-the-environment\">Navigating the environment</a> (4 minutes, 29 seconds)</p> </li> \n <li> <p><a href=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/Editor-Tips-and-Tricks\">Editor Tips and Tricks</a> (8 minutes, 45 seconds)</p> </li> \n <li> <p><a href=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/Getting-started-with-ASPNET\">Getting started with ASP.NET</a> (2 minutes, 41 seconds)</p> </li> \n <li> <p><a href=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/Getting-started-with-nodejs\">Getting started with node.js</a> (5 minutes, 33 seconds)</p> </li> \n <li> <p><a href=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/Getting-started-with-PHP\">Getting started with PHP</a> (4 minutes, 10 seconds)</p> </li> \n <li> <p><a href=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/Using-LESS-in-nodejs\">Using LESS in node.js</a> (3 minutes, 15 seconds)</p> </li> \n <li> <p><a href=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/Debugging-nodejs\">Debugging node.js</a> (2 minutes, 52 seconds)</p> </li> \n <li> <p><a href=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/Getting-started-with-TypeScript\">Getting started with TypeScript</a> (3 minutes, 48 seconds)</p> </li> \n</ul> \n<p>I'll embed some of these videos at the end of the post - not here, or you'd get distracted and you'd never read the rest of the post, right?</p> \n<p><strong>The big one</strong>: <a href=\"http://www.visualstudio.com/en-us/news/2013-nov-13-vso\">Visual Studio Online updates</a> post on the Visual Studio Online features timeline news feed thing. Can I call it a blog? I just don't know. But it's wonderful.</p> \n<p>Specifically, the <strong>Announcing Visual Studio Online “Monaco”</strong> section shows how to turn it on and get started.</p> \n<h2>Walkthrough: Editing an ASP.NET site in the browser</h2> \n<p><em>Full disclosure: I tried bumbling my way through this and made a mess, then went crying to <a href=\"http://blog.davidebbo.com/\">David Ebbo</a> for help. He set me straight.</em></p> \n<h3>Step 1: Set up a Windows Azure Web Site with git deployment</h3> \n<p>First, I'm going to create a new ASP.NET site in Visual Studio 2013 and deploy it to git. Now, I could use Visual Studio Online's git hosting - of course it works well, and works with the Visual Studio Online Monaco editor. But you'd suspect some tricky business, so I'm going to use a GitHub repository.</p> \n<p>So, I'm creating a simple ASP.NET MVC 5 application (with no authentication so I don't need to deploy a database) and pushing it to a GitHub repository.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_11h21_09\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10842274533/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_11h21_09\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5539/10842274533_5e3352d0f6.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_11h22_11\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10842282363/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_11h22_11\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7396/10842282363_cc8aba40a7.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Now I'm dropping in a standard .gitignore and initializing the repo, then pushing to GitHub.</p> \n<pre>\ngit init\ngit add .\ngit commit -m \"Initial commit\"\ngit remote add github https://github.com/jongalloway/PlayingWithMonacoEditor.git\ngit push -u github master</pre> \n<p>Now I'm going to create a new Windows Azure Web Site using that GitHub repo as the source. This has been available for a long time, but I'm showing the steps in case it's new to you.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_18h06_26\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10847100516/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_18h06_26\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3688/10847100516_a186f061c9_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_18h08_04\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10847124636/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_18h08_04\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3806/10847124636_413eaedb09_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_18h10_21\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10847225454/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_18h10_21\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3708/10847225454_8c0039eef4_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_18h16_54\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10847160185/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_18h16_54\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3760/10847160185_f615da3a8c_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<h3>Step 2: Set up for editing using Visual Studio Online</h3> \n<p>Okay, we've got a live repo and the site's deployed. Now we want to edit that source using Visual Studio Online.</p> \n<p>Here's the trick I learned from David Ebbo - you want to create another Windows Azure Web Site that's just associated with the source, not the deployment. So I head back into the portal and create a new Web Site just for source editing.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_18h20_03\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10847696613/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_18h20_03\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7338/10847696613_3e428aa7e3_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Oops, I created it in Western Europe. Good thing the internets are fast.</p> \n<p>Now, in the new site, I head over to the Configure tab and enable \"Edit in Visual Studio Online\".</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_18h41_38\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10847775773/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_18h41_38\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7301/10847775773_f4172f28ba_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Can you feel it? We're almost there. Promise.</p> \n<p>Now I head over to the Dashboard tab, and click on the <em>Edit in Visual Studio Online</em> link. Like you, I can hardly contain my excitement! What will happen?</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_14h59_14\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10844926085/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_14h59_14\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5513/10844926085_277b3296cd_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Oh, a login prompt. Hmm.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_15h00_26\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10844936995/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_15h00_26\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2890/10844936995_0722c4a61e_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Fortunately, I know my username and password. If you forget, you can reset your password in the portal, on the Dashboard tab. Okay, with that done, here's what we get:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_15h04_13\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10845320933/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_15h04_13\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7379/10845320933_cd9b853311.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Hooray! Yeah, science!</p> \n<h3>Step 3: Appeal to the hipster demographic</h3> \n<p>Wait, you're not impressed because I used IE, is that it? You wanted me to see if it worked in another browser?</p> \n<p>Yeah, maybe this is an opportunity to dust off that Mac Mini to see how it does there. Hold on, it's over here somewhere. Under the... oh, that bill's overdue. Hm. Behind the... oh, there it is. Okay!</p> \n<p>So I log back in to the Azure portal on click that same Edit In Visual Studio Online link. Hey, there it is again! I'll show this screenshot bigger this time.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_18h49_05\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10847679524/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_18h49_05\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2841/10847679524_1ec29b0ba0_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<h3>Step 4: Clone the repo</h3> \n<p>Now I click on the git icon on the left side and enter my git url, then hit clone.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_19h00_18\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10847818554/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_19h00_18\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7447/10847818554_2e9a9a7018_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>And here it goes...</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_19h01_58\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10847681795/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_19h01_58\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2807/10847681795_395d8a2fb5_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Okay, that looks really cool, and my code's visible on the left. Before I start editing, I want to make sure I can build it. In the console window, I'm going to type in \"msbuild\" and cross my fingers.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_19h09_25\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10847835916/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_19h09_25\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3791/10847835916_08b4c86ccc_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Well, we couldn't expect it to work on the first shot, could we? I...</p> \n<p>Oh, it did work. Well, hey there.</p> \n<p>So, back on my Windows box, here's what the site looks like in IE11.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_19h47_36\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10848500103/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_19h47_36\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3732/10848500103_491593edbe_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<h3>Step 5: Do some editing. That's what started this whole mess, remember?</h3> \n<p>I'm going to head back over to the Mac and edit some code. To make this interesting, I'm going to edit C#, CSHTML, CSS and JavaScript. What could go wrong?</p> \n<p>First I edit the HomeController to add something to the ViewBag.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_20h04_39\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10849250694/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_20h04_39\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5489/10849250694_0ccb281c8f_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Next I edit the CSHTML, adding in some HTML and C# changes.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_21h23_39\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10849078575/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_21h23_39\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3809/10849078575_8eb9efef42_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Finally, I make some CSS edits.</p> \n<p>I'm kind of rushing through this, but there's code completion and IntelliSense all along the way. For instance, when I start editing a CSS color, I get a dropdown with available colors.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_20h14_44\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10849254236/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_20h14_44\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5546/10849254236_02f57657ae_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>When I select a color, I can see what it will look like in the editor.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_20h15_02\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10849257044/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_20h15_02\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5543/10849257044_e690fef4ec_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Now I build to make sure it's all still good.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_20h50_22\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10849447323/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_20h50_22\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3750/10849447323_fca9e1d149_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<h3>Step 6: Deploy</h3> \n<p>And git push to deploy.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_20h51_44\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10849273044/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_20h51_44\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7389/10849273044_5b9d1e7cd5_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<h3>Step 7: Full circle, with a pirouette and a lemon twist </h3> \n<p>And hopping back to my Windows / IE 11 browser, we can see that it works.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_21h22_15\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10849502723/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_21h22_15\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7401/10849502723_c08122e585_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<h2>What's the point?</h2> \n<p>Why did we do this? What did it buy us?</p> \n<p>Well, for one thing, we grabbed an ASP.NET repo from GitHub in a browser, on a Mac, made some code changes, and deployed it. If you've got a browser (free) and a Windows Azure account (free), you can edit and deploy an ASP.NET site. You could do it on an Android tablet, on an iPad, in a boat, with a goat.</p> \n<p>Even if you're on Windows, note that the Visual Studio Online experience didn't require me to sign up for or pay anything. Visual Studio Online is free for projects with five or fewer users, and didn't make me jump through any hoops to get going. That means you can get started using it. If you want more Visual Studio Online or Windows Azure services, you can get them, but there's no roadblock to creating / editing / deploying / maintaining a site right now.</p> \n<p>Oh, and this was ASP.NET MVC 5 - so, all the new stuff works out of the box. Yes, you'd expect that, but it's worth pointing out.</p> \n<h2>Recap and regrets</h2> \n<p>Yes, we've arrived. We deployed code to GitHub, pulled it into a Windows Azure Web Site, edited it in a browser, on a Mac (demo bingo, everyone drink), and saw it automatically deployed and running in a browser. Neat.</p> \n<p>What I didn't have time to show you was all the cool features in the Monaco editor.</p> \n<p>For instance, you can click on the settings gear in the upper right corner and switch to the Visual Studio dark theme, which is so much cooler.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_21h46_07\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10849615443/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_21h46_07\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7303/10849615443_b2a206e760_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>I regret that we didn't have time to look at all of the cool editor features, like the editor commands shown when you hit ctrl+E.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_21h48_36\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10849307605/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_21h48_36\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3790/10849307605_ec7d9ffbe3_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>I wish we'd had time to look at the fancy IntelliSense features, including support for jQuery function overloads.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_21h50_33\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10849320255/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_21h50_33\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7426/10849320255_5f39bf2cac_b.jpg\" width=\"379\" height=\"64\" /></a></p> \n<p>And it would have been neat to cover the cool diff-on-commit view.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-11-13_21h52_46\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10849680643/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-11-13_21h52_46\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5514/10849680643_3c36815a82_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>But, alas, it was not to be.</p> \n<p>For those features, and many more, I recommend you check out the videos I referenced earlier. I'll even embed some of those in the next section, because this post wasn't quite long enough.</p> \n<h2></h2> \n<h2>Neat Videos You Should Watch</h2> \n<p>All of the Monaco videos are good, but these are some of my favorite.</p> \n<p>First Steps</p> \n<iframe style=\"height: 540px; width: 960px\" src=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/First-Steps/player?w=960&amp;h=540\" frameborder=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe> \n<p>Editor Tips And Tricks</p> \n<iframe style=\"height: 540px; width: 960px\" src=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/Editor-Tips-and-Tricks/player?w=960&amp;h=540\" frameborder=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe> \n<p>Getting Started With PHP</p> \n<iframe style=\"height: 540px; width: 960px\" src=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/Getting-started-with-PHP/player?w=960&amp;h=540\" frameborder=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe> \n<p>Debugging node.js</p> \n<iframe style=\"height: 540px; width: 960px\" src=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Visual-Studio-Online-Monaco/Debugging-nodejs/player?w=960&amp;h=540\" frameborder=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe> \n<p>And last, but not least, Getting started with TypeScript. \n <li><a href=\"https://github.com/aspnet/Home\">ASP.NET vNext Home repository on GitHub</a> - The place to get started with the code </li> \n</ul> \n<h2>What Is It?</h2> \n<p>In case you haven't read up on it, I'll just quote from the ASP.NET site:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>The next version of ASP.NET (“ASP.NET vNext”) has been re-designed from the ground up. The goal is to create a lean and composable .NET stack for building modern cloud-based apps.</p> \n <p>Here are some of the features of ASP.NET vNext:</p> \n <ul> \n  <li>vNext includes new cloud-optimized versions of MVC, Web API, Web Pages, SignalR, and Entity Framework. </li> \n  <li>MVC, Web API, and Web Pages will be merged into one framework, called MVC 6. The new framework removes a lot of overlap between the existing MVC and Web API frameworks. It uses a common set of abstractions for routing, action selection, filters, model binding, and so on. You can use the framework to create both UI (HTML) and web APIs. </li> \n  <li>ASP.NET vNext apps can use a cloud-optimized subset of .NET vNext. This subset is factored for server and web workloads, has a smaller footprint than the full .NET vNext, and supports side-by-side deployment. </li> \n  <li>MVC 6 has no dependency on System.Web. The result is a leaner framework, with faster startup time and lower memory consumption. </li> \n  <li>vNext will support true side-by-side deployment. If your app uses the cloud-optimized subset of .NET vNext, you can bin deploy all of your dependencies, including the .NET vNext (cloud optimized) packages. That means you can update your app without affecting other applications on the same server. </li> \n  <li>vNext is host agnostic. You can host your app in IIS, or self-host in a custom process. (Web API 2 and SignalR 2 already support self-hosting; ASP.NET vNext brings this same capability to MVC.) </li> \n  <li>Dependency injection is built into the framework. Use your preferred IoC container to register dependencies. </li> \n  <li>vNext uses the Rosyln compiler to compile code dynamically. You will be able to edit a code file, refresh the browser, and see the changes without rebuilding the project. </li> \n  <li>vNext is open source and cross platform. </li> \n </ul> \n</blockquote> \n<p>To me as a web developer, this means I get:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>All the advantages of the .NET platform (performance, stability, security, comprehensive API), and </li> \n <li>The development experience of C# and Visual Studio... with </li> \n <li>The simplicity, portability, quick dev refresh cycle and flexibility of an interpreted web framework. </li> \n</ul> \n<p>And I like the sound of that.</p> \n<h2>Source Code</h2> \n<p>All the source code and samples are published under a new <a href=\"https://github.com/aspnet\">ASP.NET organization on GitHub</a>. There are lots of interesting repos to look at; here are some top ones to get started with:</p> \n<h3><a href=\"https://github.com/aspnet/Home\">Home repository</a></h3> \n<p>This is the place to get started. The readme for this repo explains how to install and run the Hello World samples.</p> \n<h3><a title=\"https://github.com/aspnet/MusicStore\" href=\"https://github.com/aspnet/MusicStore\">Music Store sample</a></h3> \n<p>Ah, the Music Store. The team wanted some samples to validate and test vNext as they developed it, and this was one of them. I updated the source code to ASP.NET MVC 5 and threw it over the wall to them, and it seems to have held up. Cephas Lin has a <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/vnext/overview/aspnet-vnext/walkthrough-mvc-music-store\">Music Store walkthrough posted in the vNext content on the ASP.NET site</a>, and it's pretty easy to follow along.</p> \n<h3><a href=\"https://github.com/aspnet/BugTracker\">BugTracker sample</a></h3> \n<p>The BugTracker is a single page application using SignalR,&nbsp; Knockout.js and Web API. I'm pretty happy that they had a single page application as one of their validation cases from the beginning.</p> \n<h3><a title=\"https://github.com/aspnet/KRuntime\" href=\"https://github.com/aspnet/KRuntime\">KRuntime</a></h3> \n<p>If you're feeling adventurous, this it the actual runtime. It includes things like the compilation system, SDK tools, and the native CLR hosts. (parental warning advisory if stumbling across some C++ gives you nightmares)</p> \n<ul>\n <!--EndFragment-->\n</ul> \n<p><font size=\"5\">Quick Walkthrough</font></p> \n<p>I promised I'd skip the detailed walkthrough, because you really should be looking at the \"official\" ones I've linked to above. <strong>My point here is not really guide you through them, but to give you a look at what the experience is like if you're not feeling up to doing it yourself. So let's see what I can get running in 30 minutes or so (until my next meeting). If you want to follow along, read the walkthroughs.</strong></p> \n<h3>Important Notes Before We Get Started</h3> \n<p>I'm doing this on my dev machine. It runs side by side with my existing .NET and Visual Studio 2013 installs.</p> \n<p>This looks a bit fiddly because we're doing this all from the commandline. Don't worry if that's not your bag - this will all be supported via Visual Studio. This is an early preview. But, it's good that this level of control is available. Note that I'm doing all of this without firing up Visual Studio or installing any other software.</p> \n<p>You'll see the letter K pops up from time to time. This was internally called Project K before it was released. I have no idea if the k will go away now, but I kind of like it. So we've got kvm (k version manager), kre (k runtime engine), kpm (k package manager), and k (the actual bootstrapper to run our app).</p> \n<h3>The Home Repo samples</h3> \n<p>First, let's try out the <a href=\"https://github.com/aspnet/home\">ASP.NET vNext Home repo</a>. I've already got this locally, but for the purposes of science I'll pretend that I don't. Since I'm just kicking the tires here, instead of cloning the repo I'll just download the zip to my desktop, unblock if necessary, and unzip it.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-05-13_14h27_33\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/14200631213/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2014-05-13_14h27_33\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2919/14200631213_8cc6c267cf_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Here's what that gets me:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: plain; auto-links: false;\">C:\\Users\\Jon\\Desktop\\Home-master\\Home-master&gt;dir\n Volume in drive C has no label.\n Volume Serial Number is 5E2E-AE5E\n\n Directory of C:\\Users\\Jon\\Desktop\\Home-master\\Home-master\n\n05/13/2014  02:29 PM    &lt;DIR&gt;          .\n05/13/2014  02:29 PM    &lt;DIR&gt;          ..\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM               851 .gitattributes\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM               245 .gitignore\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM             1,513 CONTRIBUTING.md\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM               356 kvm.cmd\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM            17,278 kvm.ps1\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM                28 kvmsetup.cmd\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM               592 LICENSE.txt\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM               481 NuGet.Config\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM             6,390 README.md\n05/13/2014  02:29 PM    &lt;DIR&gt;          samples\n               9 File(s)         27,734 bytes\n               3 Dir(s)  11,363,086,336 bytes free</pre> \n<p>The next step in the readme tells me to execute kvmsetup.cmd, which tells me this:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: plain; auto-links: false;\">Copying file C:\\Users\\Jon\\.kre\\bin\\kvm.ps1\nCopying file C:\\Users\\Jon\\.kre\\bin\\kvm.cmd\nAdding C:\\Users\\Jon\\.kre\\bin to process PATH\nAdding C:\\Users\\Jon\\.kre\\bin to user PATH\nAdding C:\\Program Files\\KRE;%USERPROFILE%\\.kre to process KRE_HOME\nAdding C:\\Program Files\\KRE;%USERPROFILE%\\.kre to machine KRE_HOME\nPress any key to continue ...</pre> \n<p>And with that, we've got the version manager installed. Important: this is the version manager, not the runtime. We can install multiple versions of the runtime engine, and use kvm to select the active one for a project.</p> \n<p>Next, the readme tells me to install a named version of the K Runtime Engine: <code>kvm install 0.1-alpha-build-0421</code></p> \n<pre class=\"brush: plain; auto-links: false;\">C:\\Users\\Jon\\Desktop\\Home-master\\Home-master&gt;kvm install 0.1-alpha-build-0421\nDownloading KRE-svr50-x86.0.1-alpha-build-0421 from https://www.myget.org/F/aspnetvnext/api/v2/\nInstalling to C:\\Users\\Jon\\.kre\\packages\\KRE-svr50-x86.0.1-alpha-build-0421\nAdding C:\\Users\\Jon\\.kre\\packages\\KRE-svr50-x86.0.1-alpha-build-0421\\bin to process PATH</pre> \n<p>Now we've got a runtime installed, so we can run some samples. The readme recommends running the console sample first, and I think that makes sense since it's an incredibly simple app that verifies things are installed. So I cd to samples\\ConsoleApp and run kpm restore. This looks scary, but it's really fast, and it's a good thing. The idea is that instead of running on big, monolithic framework assemblies, ASP.NET vNext is grabbing a bunch of small, focused NuGet packages. </p> \n<pre class=\"brush: plain; auto-links: https://nuget.org/api/v2/FindPackagesById()?Id='System.Threading.Tasks' 1051ms\n  GET https://www.myget.org/F/aspnetvnext/api/v2/package/System.Threading.Tasks/4.0.0.0\n  OK System.Threading.Tasks 4.0.0.0\nRestore complete, 8495ms elapsed</pre> \n<p>Now I run it with k run:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: plain; auto-links: false;\">C:\\Users\\Jon\\Desktop\\Home-master\\Home-master\\samples\\ConsoleApp&gt;k run\nHello World\n\nC:\\Users\\Jon\\Desktop\\Home-master\\Home-master\\samples\\ConsoleApp&gt;</pre> \n<p>Like I said, not all that exciting. Just a quick verification check. Now that I know that's working, I'll quickly pop into one of the other sandbox samples, the HelloWeb one. Notice how simple the startup.cs file is (<a href=\"http://www.asp.net/vnext/overview/aspnet-vnext/overview\">the official walkthrough explains it in detail</a>). </p> \n<pre class=\"brush: plain; auto-links: false;\">C:\\Users\\Jon\\Desktop\\Home-master\\Home-master\\samples\\HelloWeb&gt;dir\n Volume in drive C has no label.\n Volume Serial Number is 5E2E-AE5E\n\n Directory of C:\\Users\\Jon\\Desktop\\Home-master\\Home-master\\samples\\HelloWeb\n\n05/13/2014  02:29 PM    &lt;DIR&gt;          .\n05/13/2014  02:29 PM    &lt;DIR&gt;          ..\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM           310,647 image.jpg\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM               506 project.json\n05/13/2014  01:51 PM               227 Startup.cs\n               3 File(s)        311,380 bytes\n               2 Dir(s)  11,368,951,808 bytes free\n\nC:\\Users\\Jon\\Desktop\\Home-master\\Home-master\\samples\\HelloWeb&gt;copy startup.cs con\nusing Microsoft.AspNet.Builder;\n\nnamespace KWebStartup\n{\n    public class Startup\n    {\n        public void Configure(IBuilder app)\n        {\n            app.UseStaticFiles();\n            app.UseWelcomePage();\n        }\n    }\n}        1 file(s) copied.\n\nC:\\Users\\Jon\\Desktop\\Home-master\\Home-master\\samples\\HelloWeb&gt;</pre> \n<p>Now I'll call kpm restore , just like before. This time kpm restore takes a bit longer, because there are more included packages (listed in <a href=\"https://github.com/aspnet/Home/blob/master/samples/HelloWeb/project.json\">project.json</a>) and their dependencies.</p> \n<p>And I'm ready to run it. This time, instead of k run, I'll call k web since it's a web app. If I forget and call k run, it reminds what's what:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: plain; auto-links: false;\">C:\\Users\\Jon\\Desktop\\Home-master\\Home-master\\samples\\HelloWeb&gt;k run\n'HelloWeb' does not contain a static 'Main' method suitable for an entry point</pre> \n<p>Fine, k web it is. It tells me the server's started, but how do I view it? Well, the readme tells me it's at <a href=\"http://localhost:5001\">http://localhost:5001</a>, but if I didn't know I could consult the commands section of project.json:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: plain; auto-links: false;\">\"commands\": {\n    \"web\": \"Microsoft.AspNet.Hosting server=Microsoft.AspNet.Server.WebListener server.urls=http://localhost:5001\"\n  }</pre> \n<p>Simple enough. And browsing to that gives me a cool hello world page:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-05-13_15h01_50\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/13993838169/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2014-05-13_15h01_50\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5566/13993838169_53a2e6619f_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Nerd note: That page is shown because we've got app.UseWelcomePage() in startup.cs. There are no images or css in the project because everything's contained in the emitted HTML. It's actually really impressive - it's got embedded fonts, images as data:urls, CSS, and a minimized version of jQuery (for some nice animations), so it's a 287KB HTML payload... but since it's being served locally and you're not going to run this in production, not a problem.</p> \n<p>Nerd smackdown: David Fowler reminded me that this welcome page has been in Katana for a while.</p> \n<h3>Music Store</h3> \n<p>Okay, now that I've got this stuff installed, let's see how fast I can get the Music Store sample running.</p> \n<p>Step 1: Grab the zip from <a title=\"https://github.com/aspnet/MusicStore\" href=\"https://github.com/aspnet/MusicStore\">https://github.com/aspnet/MusicStore</a>, verify it's not blocked, and unzip on desktop.</p> \n<p>Step 2: Run kpm restore.</p> \n<p>Step 3: Select the hosting opeion (Helios, SelfHost, CustomHost - explained <a href=\"https://github.com/aspnet/MusicStore\">here</a>). In this case I'll stick with selfhost, so I run k web and browse to localhost:5002:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-05-13_15h15_46\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/13993925660/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2014-05-13_15h15_46\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2897/13993925660_5110a4b5e3.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Yippee!</p> \n<p>Again, the point is that it's pretty quick and painless to get started and play with the samples; just follow the walkthroughs. If you're used to Node or Rails a lot of these steps should seem pretty familiar. If you're not and this freaks you out, don't be freaked out... this will all work smoothly from within Visual Studio in the release version. This is a preview. Take a look! Have Fun! Let us know what you think!</p> \n<h2>Videos</h2> \n<p>The best way to get up to speed is by watching the videos I mentioned earlier.</p> \n<p><iframe style=\"height: 540px; width: 960px\" src=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Events/TechEd/NorthAmerica/2014/DEV-B385/player?h=540&amp;w=960\" frameborder=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\" allowfullscreen=\"allowfullscreen\"></iframe></p> \n<p>&nbsp;</p> 09:30:59","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:30:59","title":"Top things web developers should know about the Visual Studio 2013 release","link":"https://weblogs.asp.net:443/jongalloway/top-things-web-developers-should-know-about-the-visual-studio-2013-release","description":"<p>ASP.NET and Web Tools for Visual Studio 2013 Release NotesASP.NET and Web Tools for Visual Studio 2013 Release NotesSummary for lazy readers:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Visual Studio 2013 is now available for <a href=\"http://www.microsoft.com/visualstudio/eng/downloads\">download on the Visual Studio site</a> and on <a href=\"http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-US/subscriptions/downloads/\">MSDN subscriber downloads</a>) </li> \n <li>Visual Studio 2013 installs side by side with Visual Studio 2012 and supports round-tripping between Visual Studio versions, so you can try it out without committing to a switch </li> \n <li>Visual Studio 2013 ships with the new version of ASP.NET, which includes ASP.NET MVC 5, ASP.NET Web API 2, Razor 3, Entity Framework 6 and SignalR 2.0 </li> \n <li>The new releases ASP.NET focuses on One ASP.NET, so core features and web tools work the same across the platform (e.g. adding ASP.NET MVC controllers to a Web Forms application) </li> \n <li>New core features include new templates based on Bootstrap, a new scaffolding system, and a new identity system </li> \n <li>Visual Studio 2013 is an incredible editor for web files, including HTML, CSS, JavaScript, Markdown, LESS, Coffeescript, Handlebars, Angular, Ember, Knockdown, etc. </li> \n</ul> \n<p>Top links:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Visual Studio 2013 content on the ASP.NET site are in the standard new releases area: <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/vnext\">http://www.asp.net/vnext</a> </li> \n <li><a href=\"http://www.asp.net/visual-studio/overview/2013/release-notes\">ASP.NET and Web Tools for Visual Studio 2013 Release Notes</a> </li> \n <li>Short intro videos on the new <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/visual-studio/overview/2013\">Visual Studio web editor features from Scott Hanselman and Mads Kristensen</a> </li> \n <li><a href=\"http://blogs.msdn.com/b/webdev/archive/2013/10/17/announcing-release-of-asp-net-and-web-tools-for-visual-studio-2013.aspx\">Announcing release of ASP.NET and Web Tools for Visual Studio 2013</a> post on the official .NET Web Development and Tools Blog </li> \n <li>The <a href=\"http://blogs.msdn.com/b/webdev/\">.NET Web Development and Tools Blog</a> has a lot of good post about the release</li> \n <li>Scott Guthrie's post: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/scottgu/archive/2013/10/17/announcing-the-release-of-visual-studio-2013-and-great-improvements-to-asp-net-and-entity-framework.aspx\">Announcing the Release of Visual Studio 2013 and Great Improvements to ASP.NET and Entity Framework</a> </li> \n <li>Scott Hanselman did a series of quick feature overview videos on the ASP.NET site. These are really, really well done. Watch them here: <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/visual-studio/overview/2013\">http://www.asp.net/visual-studio/overview/2013</a></li> \n</ul> \n<p>Okay, for those of you who are still with me, let's dig in a bit.</p> \n<h2>Quick web dev notes on downloading and installing Visual Studio 2013</h2> \n<p>I found Visual Studio 2013 to be a pretty fast install. According to Brian Harry's release post, <a href=\"http://blogs.msdn.com/b/bharry/archive/2013/10/17/visual-studio-2013-rtm-available.aspx\">installing over pre-release versions of Visual Studio is supported</a>.&nbsp; I've installed the release version over pre-release versions, and it worked fine.</p> \n<p>If you're only going to be doing web development, you can speed up the install if you just select Web Developer tools.</p> \n<p><a title=\"Visual Studio 2013 installer\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10329929544/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"Visual Studio 2013 installer\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3759/10329929544_4eaf8b4e42.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Of course, as a good Microsoft employee, I'll mention that you might also want to install some of those other features, like the Store apps for Windows 8 and the Windows Phone 8.0 SDK, but they do download and install a lot of other stuff (e.g. the Windows Phone SDK sets up Hyper-V and downloads several GB's of VM's). So if you're planning just to do web development for now, you can pick just the Web Developer Tools and install the other stuff later.</p> \n<p>If you've got a fast internet connection, I recommend using the web installer instead of downloading the ISO. The ISO includes all the features, whereas the web installer just downloads what you're installing.</p> \n<p>Visual Studio 2013 development settings and color theme</p> \n<p>When you start up Visual Studio, it'll prompt you to pick some defaults. These are totally up to you -whatever suits your development style - and you can change them later.</p> \n<p><a title=\"Visual Studio 2013 color theme\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10330357344/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"Visual Studio 2013 color theme\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5505/10330357344_accd88b546.jpg\" width=\"391\" height=\"300\" /></a></p> \n<p>As I said, these are completely up to you. I recommend either the Web Development or Web Development (Code Only) settings. The only real difference is that Code Only hides the toolbars, and you can switch between them using Tools / Import and Export Settings / Reset.</p> \n<h3>Web Development settings</h3> \n<p><a title=\"Web Development settings\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10330563935/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"Web Development settings\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7418/10330563935_af181417dc.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Web Development (code only) settings</p> \n<p><a title=\"Web Development (code only) settings\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10330585416/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"Web Development (code only) settings\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5547/10330585416_596ffe6103.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Usually I've just gone with Web Development (code only) in the past because I just want to focus on the code, although the Standard toolbar does make it easier to switch default web browsers. More on that later.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_09h24_56\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10330638714/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_09h24_56\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7378/10330638714_965cb1a342.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<h3>Color theme</h3> \n<p>Sigh. Okay, everyone's got their favorite colors. I alternate between Light and Dark depending on my mood, and I personally like how the low contrast on the window chrome in those themes puts the emphasis on my code rather than the tabs and toolbars. I know some people got pretty worked up over that, though, and wanted the blue theme back. I personally don't like it - it reminds me of ancient versions of Visual Studio that I don't want to think about anymore.</p> \n<p>So here's the thing: if you install Visual Studio Ultimate, it defaults to Blue. The other versions default to Light. If you use Blue, I won't criticize you - out loud, that is. You can change themes really easily - either Tools / Options / Environment / General, or the smart way: ctrl+q for quick launch, then type Theme and hit enter.</p> \n<h3>Signing in</h3> \n<p>During the first run, you'll be prompted to sign in. You don't have to - you can click the \"Not now, maybe later\" link at the bottom of that dialog. I recommend signing in, though. It's not hooked in with licensing or tracking the kind of code you write to sell you components. It is doing good things, like&nbsp; <a href=\"http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-US/library/vstudio/dn135229(v=vs.120).aspx\">syncing your Visual Studio settings between computers.</a> More about that <a href=\"http://blogs.msdn.com/b/visualstudio/archive/2013/06/28/welcome-sign-in-to-visual-studio.aspx\">here</a>. So, you don't have to, but I sure do.</p> \n<h2>Overview of shiny new things in ASP.NET land</h2> \n<p>There are a lot of good new things in ASP.NET. I'll list some of my favorite here, but you can <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/vnext\">read more on the ASP.NET site</a>.</p> \n<h2>One ASP.NET</h2> \n<p>You've heard us talk about this for a while. The idea is that options are good, but choice can be a burden. When you start a new ASP.NET project, why should you have to make a tough decision - with long-term consequences - about how your application will work? If you want to use ASP.NET Web Forms, but have the option of adding in ASP.NET MVC later, why should that be hard? It's all ASP.NET, right?</p> \n<p>Ideally, you'd just decide that you want to use ASP.NET to build sites and services, and you could use the appropriate tools (the green blocks below) as you needed them.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_09h56_49\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10331262063/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_09h56_49\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7378/10331262063_df982ef217.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>So, here it is.</p> \n<p>When you create a new ASP.NET application, you just create an ASP.NET application.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_09h59_58\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10331142464/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_09h59_58\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7457/10331142464_d1147a5268.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Next, you can pick from some templates to get you started... but these are different. They're not \"painful decision\" templates, they're just some starting pieces. And, most importantly, you can mix and match. I can pick a \"mostly\" Web Forms template, but include MVC and Web API folders and core references.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_10h05_27\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10331394573/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_10h05_27\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5488/10331394573_69edea75c6.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>If you've tried to mix and match in the past, you're probably aware that it was possible, but not pleasant. ASP.NET MVC project files contained special project type GUIDs, so you'd only get controller scaffolding support in a Web Forms project if you manually edited the csproj file. Features in one stack didn't work in others. Project templates were painful choices. That's no longer the case. Hooray! </p> \n<p>I just did a demo in a presentation last week where I created a new Web Forms + MVC + Web API site, built a model, scaffolded MVC and Web API controllers with EF Code First, add data in the MVC view, viewed it in Web API, then added a GridView to the Web Forms Default.aspx page and bound it to the Model. In about 5 minutes. Sure, it's a simple example, but it's great to be able to share code and features across the whole ASP.NET family.</p> \n<h2>Authentication</h2> \n<p>In the past, authentication was built into the templates. So, for instance, there was an ASP.NET MVC 4 Intranet Project template which created a new ASP.NET MVC 4 application that was preconfigured for Windows Authentication. All of that authentication stuff was built into each template, so they varied between the stacks, and you couldn't reuse them. You didn't see a lot of changes to the authentication options, since they required big changes to a bunch of project templates.</p> \n<p>Now, the new project dialog includes a common authentication experience. When you hit the Change Authentication button, you get some common options that work the same way regardless of the template or reference settings you've made. These options work on all ASP.NET frameworks, and all hosting environments (IIS, IIS Express, or OWIN for self-host)</p> \n<p>The default is Individual User Accounts:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_10h33_15\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10331628534/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_10h33_15\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2882/10331628534_a671108fd5.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>This is the standard \"create a local account, using username / password or OAuth\" thing; however, it's all built on the new Identity system. More on that in a second.</p> \n<p>The one setting that has some configuration to it is Organizational Accounts, which lets you configure authentication using Active Directory, Windows Azure Active Directory, or Office 365.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_10h46_50\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10331817455/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_10h46_50\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2886/10331817455_6a8587930d.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<h2>Identity</h2> \n<p>There's a new identity system. We've taken the best parts of the previous ASP.NET Membership and Simple Identity systems, rolled in a lot of feedback and made big enhancements to support important developer concerns like unit testing and extensiblity.</p> \n<p>I've written long posts about ASP.NET identity, and I'll do it again. Soon. This is not that post. The short version is that I think we've finally got just the right Identity system. Some of my favorite features:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>There are simple, sensible defaults that work well - you can File / New / Run / Register / Login, and everything works. </li> \n <li>It supports standard username / password as well as external authentication (OAuth, etc.). </li> \n <li>It's easy to customize without having to re-implement an entire provider. It's built using pluggable pieces, rather than one large monolithic system. </li> \n <li>It's built using interfaces like IUser and IRole that allow for unit testing, dependency injection, etc. </li> \n <li>You can easily add user profile data (e.g. URL, twitter handle, birthday). You just add properties to your ApplicationUser model and they'll automatically be persisted. </li> \n <li>Complete control over how the identity data is persisted. By default, everything works with Entity Framework Code First, but it's built to support changes from small (modify the schema) to big (use another ORM, store your data in a document database or in the cloud or in XML or in the EXIF data of your desktop background or whatever). </li> \n <li>It's configured via OWIN. More on OWIN and Katana later, but the fact that it's built using OWIN means it's portable. </li> \n</ul> \n<p>You can find out more in the <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/aspnet/overview/authentication-and-identity\">Authentication and Identity section of the ASP.NET site</a> (and lots more content will be going up there soon).</p> \n<h2>New Bootstrap based project templates</h2> \n<p>The new project templates are built using Bootstrap 3. <a href=\"http://getbootstrap.com/\">Bootstrap</a> (formerly Twitter Bootstrap) is a front-end framework that brings a lot of nice benefits:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>It's responsive, so your projects will automatically scale to device width using CSS media queries. For example, menus are full size on a desktop browser, but on narrower screens you automatically get a mobile-friendly menu. </li> \n <li>The built-in Bootstrap styles make your standard page elements (headers, footers, buttons, form inputs, tables etc.) look nice and modern. </li> \n <li>Bootstrap is themeable, so you can reskin your whole site by dropping in a new Bootstrap theme. Since Bootstrap is pretty popular across the web development community, this gives you a large and rapidly growing variety of templates (free and paid) to choose from. </li> \n <li>Bootstrap also includes a lot of very useful things: components (like progress bars and badges), useful <a href=\"http://getbootstrap.com/components/#glyphicons\">glyphicons</a>, and some jQuery plugins for tooltips, dropdowns, carousels, etc.). </li> \n</ul> \n<p>Here's a look at how the responsive part works. When the page is full screen, the menu and header are optimized for a wide screen display:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_12h14_50\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10333317854/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_12h14_50\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7333/10333317854_1c4e65e969.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>When I shrink the page down (this is all based on page width, not useragent sniffing) the menu turns into a nice mobile-friendly dropdown:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_12h15_12\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10333498983/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_12h15_12\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3698/10333498983_cc37fdf536.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>For a quick example, I grabbed a new free theme off <a href=\"http://bootswatch.com/\">bootswatch.com</a>. For simple themes, you just need to download the boostrap.css file and replace the /content/bootstrap.css file in your project.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_12h27_44\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10333348956/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_12h27_44\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5503/10333348956_d34447d214.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Now when I refresh the page, I've got a new theme:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_12h27_33\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10333327544/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_12h27_33\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3700/10333327544_a506aa8e72.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<h2>Scaffolding</h2> \n<p>The big change in scaffolding is that it's one system that works across ASP.NET. You can create a new Empty Web project or Web Forms project and you'll get the Scaffold context menus.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_12h36_14\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10333443175/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_12h36_14\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3769/10333443175_5f83991f24_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>For release, we've got MVC 5 and Web API 2 controllers. We had a preview of Web Forms scaffolding in the preview releases, but they weren't fully baked for RTM. Look for them in a future update, expected pretty soon.</p> \n<p>This scaffolding system wasn't just changed to work across the ASP.NET frameworks, it's also built to enable future extensibility. That's not in this release, but should also hopefully be out soon.</p> \n<h2>Project Readme page</h2> \n<p>This is a small thing, but I really like it. When you create a new project, you get a Project_Readme.html page that's added to the root of your project and opens in the Visual Studio built-in browser.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_14h25_35\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10335264663/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_14h25_35\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3750/10335264663_304a16c2f9_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>I love it.</p> \n<p>A long time ago, when you created a new project we just dumped it on you and left you scratching your head about what to do next. Not ideal.</p> \n<p>Then we started adding a bunch of Getting Started information to the new project templates. That told you what to do next, but you had to delete all of that stuff out of your website. It doesn't belong there. Not ideal.</p> \n<p>This is a simple HTML file that's not integrated into your project code at all. You can delete it if you want. But, it shows a lot of helpful links that are current for the project you just created. In the future, if we add new wacky project types, they can create readme docs with specific information on how to do appropriately wacky things.</p> \n<p>Side note: I really like that they used the internal browser in Visual Studio to show this content rather than popping open an HTML page in the default browser. I hate that. It's annoying. If you're doing that, I hope you'll stop. What if some unnamed person has 40 or 90 tabs saved in their browser session? When you pop open your \"Thanks for installing my Visual Studio extension!\" page, all eleventy billion tabs start up and I wish I'd never installed your thing. Be like these guys and pop stuff Visual Studio specific HTML docs in the Visual Studio browser.</p> \n<h2>ASP.NET MVC 5</h2> \n<p>The biggest change with ASP.NET MVC 5 is that it's no longer a separate project type. It integrates well with the rest of ASP.NET.</p> \n<p>In addition to that and the other common features we've already looked at (Bootstrap templates, Identity, authentication), here's what's new for ASP.NET MVC.</p> \n<h3>Attribute routing</h3> \n<p>ASP.NET MVC now supports attribute routing, thanks to a contribution by Tim McCall, the author of <a href=\"http://attributerouting.net/\">http://attributerouting.net</a>. With attribute routing you can specify your routes by annotating your actions and controllers. This supports some pretty complex, customized routing scenarios, and it allows you to keep your route information right with your controller actions if you'd like.</p> \n<p>Here's a controller that includes an action whose method name is Hiding, but I've used AttributeRouting to configure it to /spaghetti/with-nesting/where-is-waldo</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">public class SampleController : Controller\n{\n\n    [Route(\"spaghetti/with-nesting/where-is-waldo\")]\n    public string Hiding() \n    {\n        return \"You found me!\";\n    }\n}</pre> \n<p>I enable that in my RouteConfig.cs, and I can use that in conjunction with my other MVC routes like this:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">public class RouteConfig\n{\n    public static void RegisterRoutes(RouteCollection routes)\n    {\n        routes.IgnoreRoute(\"{resource}.axd/{*pathInfo}\");\n\n        routes.MapMvcAttributeRoutes();\n\n        routes.MapRoute(\n            name: \"Default\",\n            url: \"{controller}/{action}/{id}\",\n            defaults: new { controller = \"Home\", action = \"Index\", id = UrlParameter.Optional }\n        );\n    }\n}</pre> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_14h06_56\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10334803914/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_14h06_56\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5536/10334803914_bb41127fc4.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>You can read <a href=\"http://blogs.msdn.com/b/webdev/archive/2013/10/17/attribute-routing-in-asp-net-mvc-5.aspx\">more about Attribute Routing in ASP.NET MVC 5 here</a>.</p> \n<h3>Filter enhancements</h3> \n<p>There are two new additions to filters: Authentication Filters and Filter Overrides.</p> \n<p>Authentication filters are a new kind of filter in ASP.NET MVC that run prior to authorization filters in the ASP.NET MVC pipeline and allow you to specify authentication logic per-action, per-controller, or globally for all controllers. Authentication filters process credentials in the request and provide a corresponding principal. Authentication filters can also add authentication challenges in response to unauthorized requests.</p> \n<p>Override filters let you change which filters apply to a given action method or controller. Override filters specify a set of filter types that should not be run for a given scope (action or controller). This allows you to configure filters that apply globally but then exclude certain global filters from applying to specific actions or controllers.</p> \n<h2>ASP.NET Web API 2</h2> \n<p>ASP.NET Web API 2 includes a lot of new features.</p> \n<h3>Attribute Routing</h3> \n<p>ASP.NET Web API supports the same attribute routing system that's in ASP.NET MVC 5. You can read more about the Attribute Routing features in Web API in this <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/web-api/overview/web-api-routing-and-actions/attribute-routing-in-web-api-2\">article</a>.</p> \n<h3>OAuth 2.0</h3> \n<p>ASP.NET Web API picks up OAuth 2.0 support, using security middleware running on OWIN (discussed below). This is great for features like authenticated Single Page Applications.</p> \n<h3>OData Improvements</h3> \n<p>ASP.NET Web API now has full OData support. That required adding in some of the most powerful operators: $select, $expand, $batch and $value. You can read more about <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/web-api/overview/odata-support-in-aspnet-web-api/using-$select,-$expand,-and-$value\">OData operator support in this article by Mike Wasson</a>.</p> \n<p>Lots more</p> \n<p>There's a huge list of other features, including CORS (cross-origin request sharing), IHttpActionResult, IHttpRequestContext, and more. I think the best overview is in the <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/visual-studio/overview/2013/release-notes#TOC11\">release notes</a>.</p> \n<h2>OWIN and Katana</h2> \n<p>I've written about <a href=\"https://weblogs.asp.net:443/jongalloway/weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2013/05/30/getting-up-to-speed-with-katana.aspx\">OWIN and Katana recently</a>. I'm a big fan.</p> \n<p>OWIN is the Open Web Interfaces for .NET. It's a spec, like HTML or HTTP, so you can't install OWIN. The benefit of OWIN is that it's a community specification, so anyone who implements it can plug into the ASP.NET stack, either as middleware or as a host.</p> \n<p>Katana is the Microsoft implementation of OWIN. It leverages OWIN to wire up things like authentication, handlers, modules, IIS hosting, etc., so ASP.NET can host OWIN components and Katana components can run in someone else's OWIN implementation.</p> \n<p>Howard Dierking just wrote a cool article in MSDN magazine describing Katana in depth: <a href=\"http://msdn.microsoft.com/en-us/magazine/dn451439.aspx\">Getting Started with the Katana Project</a>. He had an interesting example showing an OWIN based pipeline which leveraged SignalR, ASP.NET Web API and NancyFx components in the same stack.</p> \n<p><img src=\"http://i.msdn.microsoft.com/dn451439.Dierking_Figure11_hires(en-us,MSDN.10).png\" /></p> \n<p>If this kind of thing makes sense to you, that's great. If it doesn't, don't worry, but keep an eye on it. You're going to see some cool things happen as a result of ASP.NET becoming more and more pluggable.</p> \n<h2>Visual Studio Web Tools</h2> \n<p>Okay, this stuff's just crazy. Visual Studio has been adding some nice web dev features over the past few years, but they've really cranked it up for this release. </p> \n<p>Visual Studio is by far my favorite code editor for all web files: CSS, HTML, JavaScript, and lots of popular libraries. </p> \n<p><strong>Stop thinking of Visual Studio as a big editor that you only use to write back-end code. Stop editing HTML and CSS in Notepad (or Sublime, Notepad++, etc.). Visual Studio starts up in under 2 seconds on a modern computer with an SSD. Misspelling HTML attributes or your CSS classes or jQuery or Angular syntax is stupid. It doesn't make you a better developer, it makes you a silly person who wastes time.</strong></p> \n<h3>Browser Link</h3> \n<p>Browser Link is a real-time, two-way connection between Visual Studio and all connected browsers. It's only attached when you're running locally, in debug, but it applies to any and all connected browser, including emulators.</p> \n<p>You may have seen demos that showed the browsers refreshing based on changes in the editor, and I'll agree that's pretty cool. But it's really just the start. It's a two-way connection, and it's built for extensiblity. That means you can write extensions that push information from your running application (in IE, Chrome, a mobile emulator, etc.) back to Visual Studio. Mads and team have showed off some demonstrations where they enabled edit mode in the browser which updated the source HTML back on the browser. It's also possible to look at how the rendered HTML performs, check for compatibility issues, watch for unused CSS classes, the sky's the limit.</p> \n<iframe style=\"height: 540px; width: 960px\" src=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Blogs/ASP-NET-Site-Videos/Visual-Studio-2013-Web-Editor-Features-Browser-Link/player?w=960&amp;h=540\" frameborder=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe> \n<h3>New HTML editor</h3> \n<p>The previous HTML editor had a lot of old code that didn't allow for improvements. The team rewrote the HTML editor to take advantage of the new(ish) extensibility features in Visual Studio, which then allowed them to <a href=\"http://blogs.msdn.com/b/webdev/archive/2013/09/30/html-editing-features-in-visual-studio-2013-rc.aspx\">add in all kinds of features</a> - things like CSS Class and ID IntelliSense (so you type style=\"\" and get a list of classes and ID's for your project), smart indent based on how your document is formatted, JavaScript reference auto-sync, etc.</p> \n<p>Here's a 3 minute tour from Mads Kristensen.</p> \n<iframe style=\"height: 540px; width: 960px\" src=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Blogs/ASP-NET-Site-Videos/Visual-Studio-2013-Web-Editor-Features-HTML-Editor/player?w=960&amp;h=540\" frameborder=\"0\" scrolling=\"no\"></iframe> \n<h3>Integrated Windows Azure Web Site creation and publishing</h3> \n<p>The <a href=\"http://manage.windowsazure.com\">Windows Azure portal</a> is good as websites go, but it's another step to have to go to the portal to create a site, then download the publish profile, then import it into my site. It's like ten clicks or something and it just gets really fatiguing and sometimes I need a nap.</p> \n<p>They've updated the Server Explorer in Visual Studio 2013 so I can just right-click on the Windows Azure node to create a site. Then when I'm publishing, I can directly import the site publish profile and go. That means I can create a new Windows Azure Web Site, with a free 20 MB SQL Database, and publish it to Windows Azure all without leaving Visual Studio. That's really nice.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2013-10-17_15h56_26\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/10336195204/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2013-10-17_15h56_26\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5504/10336195204_4ef39abec8_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>More about that on this post: <a href=\"http://blogs.msdn.com/b/webdev/archive/2013/09/11/creating-new-windows-azure-web-site-from-visual-studio-2013-rc.aspx\">Creating New Windows Azure Web Site from Visual Studio 2013 RC</a></p> \n<h3>Lots more Visual Studio web dev features</h3> \n<p>That's just a sampling - there's a ton of great features for JavaScript editing, CSS editing, publishing, and Page Inspector (which shows real-time rendering of your page inside Visual Studio). <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/visual-studio\">Here are some more short videos</a> showing those features.</p> \n<p>Lots, lots more</p> \n<p>Okay, that's just a summary, and it's still quite a bit. Head on over to <a href=\"http://asp.net/vnext\">http://asp.net/vnext</a> for more information, and download Visual Studio 2013 now to get started!</p> \n<div class=\"feedflare\"> \n <a 09:30:59","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:30:59","title":"How to Talk Yourself out of your New Year’s Blogging Resolution… One Day At A Time","link":"https://weblogs.asp.net:443/jongalloway/how-to-talk-yourself-out-of-your-new-year-s-blogging-resolution-one-day-at-a-time","description":"<p>It’s a new year! Even though years are an arbitrary construct and all that, you feel reinvigorated! Alive! Ready to take on the world! And you’ve read all those posts and tweets and instatumblrs about blogging! They’re pretty compelling, really, and you’re in a suggestible state of mind. You’re thinking about taking the plunge.</p> \n<p>Frankly, you lack the tools and experience to talk yourself out of this one. Perhaps I can be of assistance. I’ve got years of real world practice in talking myself out of blogging, and I’m here to share that knowledge with you.</p> \n<h2>Procrastination Is Key</h2> \n<p>Spoiler alert: It all comes down to putting off to tomorrow what you could do today. But for good reasons! Always good reasons. That’s how you can sell it to yourself… it’s all for a good cause, really!</p> \n<h2>Other People Have Blogged, Are Blogging, Or Will Blog This Anyway</h2> \n<p>There’s only room for a limited amount of information in the universe. Some scientific estimates [<a href=\"https://arxiv.org/pdf/quant-ph/0110141v1.pdf\">pdf</a>] cap us at figures as low as 10<sup>90</sup> bits! So we certainly don’t want any duplication whatsoever. Ergo, vis a vis, concordantly, if it’s at all possible that someone else might have blogged this kind of thing at some point, probably better not to muddy the waters or whatever.</p> \n<h2>Other, More Informed Experts Should Take This One</h2> \n<p>Okay, maybe nobody’s bloggerated this one up just yet, but this is all very technical and should really be handled by certified experts. We’re sharing technical information here, it had better be just right.</p> \n<p><strong>Experience Bonus:</strong> The more actual experience you have, the more you know that you don’t know. You also, if you’re lucky, get to come into contact with really quite actually brilliant people who know thing. Now, if you’re lucky, eventually you’ll know people who are, collectively, smarter than you at every possible subject area and you can just sit and watch them talk about all the things. If you’re really lucky, you’ll get to meet, online or even in person, <em>the</em> worldwide expert of a few things. How could you presume to blog on <em>their</em> topic?</p> \n<h2>Needs More Playtesting</h2> \n<p>Okay, you’ve actually found a cool thing and nobody’s talking about it yet. But remember that cool utility you were excited about a few years ago? Are you still excited about it now? Moved on, right? How about that super interesting technique of injecting dependencies directly into your spleen using pure ASM? It destroyed your spleen and you’re on spleen replacement pills now, aren’t you, smart guy? The truth is that absolutely anything interesting you know probably needs a little more testing before you recommend it. At least another week or two (wash rinse repeat).</p> \n<h2>Professionalize This</h2> \n<p>Look, if you’re going to write a blog post these days it’s got to follow best practices. Are your code samples <em>above reproach</em>? Are you sure??? Have you read the twitters and the hacker news comments lately? I don’t care if you’re writing “Introduction to Lego Mindstorms For Toddlers”, it had better include unit tests in a pure functional language, today’s top front end framework, and a lot of scary stuff you can’t remember right now but you know is vitally important! And have you considered scalability? What about security??? You know security is important, right? Don’t you? What is wrong with you??? Probably better sit this one out.</p> \n<p><strong>Experience Bonus:</strong> If you’ve happened to write any books, articles, official documentation or official announcement blog post, you’ve got a head start here. You’ve had editors review and critique your work. You’ve seen how the code and text you were so proud of on the first draft had obvious errors that editors and/or commenters immediately discovered. This draft is probably wrong, buddy. Better give it a day and come back to proof it again.</p> \n<h2>Those Tweets Tho</h2> \n<p>Look, here’s a loophole! Writing a blog post is hard work, and a lot of it’s really just filler, right? You can probably get the point across in a single tweet.</p> \n<p>1/ Or, if not one tweet…</p> \n<h2>I’ll Blog It When It Ships</h2> \n<p>Whoa! You just wrote some cool code, it all works, and this is definitely worth sharing!</p> \n<p>Slow down, tiger. Let’s wait until this thing actually gets through QA, user testing, and deployment. There have to be some bugs, right? Let’s wait and blog the real deal. Plus, we’re really quite busy now! After we ship, there will be time for low priority things like blogging and smelling the flowers. Wait until we ship, then things will totally calm down and we’ll do all the things we want to do now but don’t have time for.</p> \n<p>This works great for cool demonstrations you write up for a tech presentation, too! You’re way too busy preparing for the presentation right now, and that’s really it would be irresponsible to take time away to blog it up right now, but right after the conference, obviously, there will be abundant time!</p> \n<p>And also, some of this stuff might even be kind of secret until the ship date! Loose lips and all that, old boy.</p> \n<p>The beauty of this excuse, as you know deep in your heart, is that as soon as magical ship day hits, something else will come up, and you’ll never actually get that magical blogging down day.</p> \n<h2>Magnificent&nbsp; Debut [or] Don’t Call It A Comeback</h2> \n<p>Okay, logic has failed and you still want to blog. There’s hope for you yet.</p> \n<p>Fine, you’ll write some blogerisms, but it can’t be just anything! You have to come out swinging! Billions of people are watching, you can’t enter the fray with some random gibberish! The first one (or, in the restart scenario, the first <em>new</em> one) needs to be plus one insightful! Don’t ship it ‘til it’s ready! Iterate until the urge passes.</p> \n<p>And to do this right, you really should have a full code sample. Or, better yet, a repo. Well, probably what you really want is an open source project, right?</p> \n<h2>Off Topic!</h2> \n<p>Sure, sometimes you just want to write about some new random thing that’s not strictly in the topic area you normally write about. Let’s just keep that to yourself, then, shall we?</p> \n<h2>Market Timing</h2> \n<p>Hmm. None of the above defenses have helped, and you’re actually about to write a technical blog post. Fine, fine, you should totally go ahead with this… but… well, I don’t want to be a jerk, but you know it’s Monday, right? Nobody reads blogs on Monday. They’re all just coming back to work. Tuesday’s maybe okay, but you might want to wait until Wednesday.</p> \n<p>Hey, it’s Wednesday, but things are actually pretty busy with work and stuff. Maybe this afternoon… Oh, hey, I know today was busy and all, but you totally don’t want to post in the late afternoon! Do you even know about time zones? People in Europe are sleeping now and you’re about to post? Wait until tomorrow.</p> \n<p>Um, good morning. It’s Thursday morning. Dumb time to post. You really want to wait until earlier in the week for maximum hits and all that.</p> \n<p>Hello. Happy Friday / Saturday / Sunday. Have a great day, but don’t even think about posting today! Nobody’s going to read it now!</p> \n<p>Oh, and this works really well with blog posts about a software or product release! If you can’t post it on the day of the release, game over man.</p> \n<h2>Okay, Not Really</h2> \n<p>None of these are really good reasons. I’ll give my rebuttals, but I’m of course interested in yours in the comments or on Friendster.</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Other People Have Blogged, Are Blogging, Or Will Blog This Anyway</li> \n <ul> \n  <li><a href=\"http://johnnycoder.com/blog/2008/06/18/unoriginal-thoughts-bears-repeating/\">Unoriginal Thoughts Bear Repeating</a>.</li> \n  <li>Usually every blog has a new / different / updated take.</li> \n  <li>Your blog is <strong>your</strong> personal record. You can review your previous notes, it’ll be there even if other blogs go away, etc.</li> \n  <li>10<sup>90</sup> bits is actually quite a lot.</li> \n </ul> \n <li>Other, More Informed Experts Should Take This One \n  <ul> \n   <li>One of the best ways to learn more is to risk being accidentally stupid on the internet. Do your research, ask an expert to review if really necessary,&nbsp;add a disclaimer that you're not an expert, but share what you <em>think</em> you know.</li> \n  </ul> </li> \n <li>Needs More Playtesting</li> \n <ul> \n  <li>I actually think there’s a healthy balance here. I think “Hey, look at this thing I just found and might install or not” probably goes in a Tweet, but maybe set a reminder to get back to it in a week to blog your fresh experiences?</li> \n  <li>There’s always the edit button. If you try something for a while and it doesn’t work out or you find something better, update your post to explain why.</li> \n </ul> \n <li>Professionalize This</li> \n <ul> \n  <li>I think the best way to handle this is separating the decide and do decisions as much as possible, then time boxing. Is this post topic worthwhile? How many hours of investment is it worth? Great, I’ll give myself 2 hours, then out it goes.</li> \n </ul> \n <li>Those Tweets Tho</li> \n <ul> \n  <li>Not sure here. I think the kinds of conversations I have on Twitter are great for exploring a topic, but when a conversation generates some interesting response, it’s time to put it on the //toblog list.</li> \n  <li>I’m just now checking my top tweets on favstar.fm and <a href=\"https://analytics.twitter.com/\">Twitter Analytics</a>, and there are probably a few other ways to dig up past interesting conversations if needed.</li> \n </ul> \n <li>I’ll Blog It When It Ships</li> \n <ul> \n  <li>One thing that’s worked for me here is to start writing the blog post up in rough form as I go, so it’s quick and easy to post immediately afterwards.</li> \n </ul> \n <li>Off Topic!</li> \n <ul> \n  <li>I think this is really a judgement call. I know personally that I’m rarely offended by the occasional “here’s a neat thing” post from likeminded nerds. In fact, I usually like them. Sure, if you go overboard it’s too much, but give yourself some leeway.</li> \n </ul> \n <li>Magnificent&nbsp; Debut [or] Don’t Call It A Comeback</li> \n <ul> \n  <li>It’s important to remember that a blog is a “web log”. It’s not a big deal. <br /><img width=\"195\" height=\"212\" src=\"http://i.imgur.com/gZfPEFU.jpg\" /></li> \n  <li>I think it’s easier to break the ice – again, really just with myself – with a non-technical post. Hmm, like this one.</li> \n </ul> \n</ul> \n<p>Thoughts? What excuses did I miss? Have I helped talk you out of and / or back into blogging?</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\"> \n <a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/jongalloway?a=F4jjUx7gmGs:EdDhgrhGt7c:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img
