09:30:59","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:30:59","title":"Looking at ASP.NET MVC 5.1 and Web API 2.1 - Part 3 - Bootstrap and JavaScript enhancements","link":"https://weblogs.asp.net:443/jongalloway/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-3-bootstrap-and-javascript-enhancements","description":"<p>This is part 3 of a 4 part series covering some of the new features in the ASP.NET MVC 5.1 and Web API 2.1 releases.</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Part 1: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/21/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-1-overview-and-enums.aspx\" mce_href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/21/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-1-overview-and-enums.aspx\">Overview and Enums</a> </li> \n <li>Part 2: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/24/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-2-attribute-routing-with-custom-constraints.aspx\" mce_href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/24/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-2-attribute-routing-with-custom-constraints.aspx\">Attribute Routing with Custom Constraints</a> </li> \n <li>Part 3: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/28/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-3-bootstrap-and-javascript-enhancements.aspx\">Bootstrap and JavaScript enhancements</a> </li> \n <li>Part 4: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/02/26/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-4-web-api-help-pages-bson-and-global-error-handling.aspx\">Web API Help Pages, BSON, and Global Error Handling</a></li> \n</ul> \n<p>In this post, we'll be focusing on some client-side improvements to ASP.NET MVC 5.1.</p> \n<p>As a reminder if you haven't read the first post, these updates are currently delivered via a NuGet update to your existing ASP.NET MVC 5 / Web API 2 applications. They'll be part of the File / New Project templates included in an upcoming Visual Studio update.</p> \n<h2>EditorFor now supports passing HTML attributes - Great for Bootstrap</h2> \n<p>The new ASP.NET project templates all include Bootstrap themes. Bootstrap uses custom class names for everything - styling, components, layout, behavior. That made it frustrating that you couldn't pass classes down to the Html.EditorFor HTML helper: you either had to use specific HTML Helpers like Html.TextBoxFor (which do allow you to pass HTML attributes, but don't benefit from some of the other nice features in HTML.EditorFor, like Data Attribute support for display and input validation) or give up on using the Bootstrap classes and style things yourself.</p> \n<p>In the 5.1 release, you can now pass HTML attributes as an additional parameter to Html.EditorFor, allowing you to get the best of both. Here's an example of why that's useful.</p> \n<p>In the first post in the series, we scaffolded a simple create controller and associated views. The Create view ended up looking like this:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-01-28_10h51_55\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12193032655/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12193032655/\"><img alt=\"2014-01-28_10h51_55\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2890/12193032655_8a37eaee04_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2890/12193032655_8a37eaee04_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>That's okay, but it's not taking advantage of any of the Bootstrap form styling (e.g. focus indication, element sizing, groups, etc.) and it won't do anything special with custom Bootstrap themes. A great start would be just to add the \"form-control\" class to the form elements. That just involves changing from this:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">@Html.EditorFor(model =&gt; model.FirstName)</pre> \n<p>to this:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">@Html.EditorFor(model =&gt; model.FirstName, new { htmlAttributes = new { @class = \"form-control\" }, })</pre> \n<p>When I make that update to the textboxes, I get this view:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-01-28_00h51_07\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12186423094/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12186423094/\"><img alt=\"2014-01-28_00h51_07\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2850/12186423094_8976fc8c40_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2850/12186423094_8976fc8c40_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>You'll notice some subtle improvements, like the focus highlight on the FirstName field, nicer textbox size and validation layout for Age, etc. These are just really simple things with a&nbsp; very basic model, but they give a quick idea of the improvement here.</p> \n<p>Also nice is that I can pass the attributes on Html.EditorFor when displaying the entire model. Here I've updated the entire form section to just use one EditorFor call, passing in the model:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false; highlight: [10];\">@using (Html.BeginForm()) \n{\n    @Html.AntiForgeryToken()\n    \n    &lt;div class=\"form-horizontal\"&gt;\n        &lt;h4&gt;Person&lt;/h4&gt;\n        &lt;hr /&gt;\n        @Html.ValidationSummary(true)\n        @Html.EditorFor(model =&gt; model, new { htmlAttributes = new { @class = \"form-control\" }, })\n        &lt;div class=\"form-group\"&gt;\n            &lt;div class=\"col-md-offset-2 col-md-10\"&gt;\n                &lt;input type=\"submit\" value=\"Create\" class=\"btn btn-default\" /&gt;\n            &lt;/div&gt;\n        &lt;/div&gt;\n    &lt;/div&gt;\n}</pre> \n<p>Note that to make sure the Id property didn't display and to use the custom radio enum display template (as explained in the first post in the series), I added two annotations to my model. Here's how the model and associated Enum look:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false; highlight: [3,5];\">public class Person\n{\n    [ScaffoldColumn(false)]\n    public int Id { get; set; }\n    [UIHint(\"Enum-radio\")]\n    public Salutation Salutation { get; set; }\n    public string FirstName { get; set; }\n    public string LastName { get; set; }\n    public int Age { get; set; }\n}\n//I guess technically these are called honorifics\npublic enum Salutation : byte\n{\n    [Display(Name = \"Mr.\")]   Mr,\n    [Display(Name = \"Mrs.\")]  Mrs,\n    [Display(Name = \"Ms.\")]   Ms,\n    [Display(Name = \"Dr.\")]   Doctor,\n    [Display(Name = \"Prof.\")] Professor,\n    Sir, \n    Lady, \n    Lord\n}</pre> \n<p>That gives me the exact same output as shown above (the second, nicer screenshot). What's cool there is that the EditorFor method passed that form-control class to each element in the form, so each input tag got the form-control class. That means that I could apply additional Bootstrap classes, as well as my own custom classes, in that same call:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">        @Html.EditorFor(model =&gt; model, new { htmlAttributes = new { @class = \"form-control input-sm my-custom-class\" }, })</pre> \n<p>You can see the <a href=\"https://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/f5a8ab8dd751b6d046f55dfdf0138bda202bcdbf\" mce_href=\"https://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/f5a8ab8dd751b6d046f55dfdf0138bda202bcdbf\">code changes and associated tests for this EditorFor change on this commit on CodePlex</a>.</p> \n<h2>Client-side validation for MinLength and MaxLength</h2> \n<p>This is a pretty straightforward one: we had client-side validation for StringLength before, but not for MinLength and MaxLength. Personally, I feel like it's a tossup on which to use - StringLength lets you set both min and max and is more widely supported, but MinLength and MaxLength allow you to specify them separately and give different validation messages for each. Regardless, the good news is that whichever you use, they're both supported on both server and client.</p> \n<p>To test that out, we'll add some MinLength and MaxLength attributes to our Person class.</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false; highlight: [8,9,12,13];\">public class Person\n{\n    [ScaffoldColumn(false)]\n    public int Id { get; set; }\n    [UIHint(\"Enum-radio\")]\n    public Salutation Salutation { get; set; }\n    [Display(Name = \"First Name\")]\n    [MinLength(3, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be at least {1} characters long\")]\n    [MaxLength(100, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be no more than {1} characters\")]\n    public string FirstName { get; set; }\n    [Display(Name = \"Last Name\")]\n    [MinLength(3, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be at least {1} characters long\")]\n    [MaxLength(100, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be no more than {1} characters\")]\n    public string LastName { get; set; }\n    public int Age { get; set; }\n}</pre> \n<p>I get immediate feedback on what the website thinks of a potential stage name I've been considering:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-01-28_14h45_16\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12196896086/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12196896086/\"><img alt=\"2014-01-28_14h45_16\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7443/12196896086_9043699fb8_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7443/12196896086_9043699fb8_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Here's the <a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/1476\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/1476\">link to the work item</a>, and here's <a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/1eb13543f730431a2f20abe64bb8ff5df76d5941\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/1eb13543f730431a2f20abe64bb8ff5df76d5941\">the code diff for the commit</a>.</p> \n<h2>Three small but useful fixes to Unobtrusive Ajax</h2> \n<p>There are a <a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/list/advanced?keyword=ajax&amp;status=Resolved&amp;type=All&amp;priority=All&amp;release=v5.1%20Preview|v5.1%20RTM&amp;assignedTo=All&amp;component=All&amp;sortField=Votes&amp;sortDirection=Descending&amp;page=0&amp;reasonClosed=All\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/list/advanced?keyword=ajax&amp;status=Resolved&amp;type=All&amp;priority=All&amp;release=v5.1%20Preview|v5.1%20RTM&amp;assignedTo=All&amp;component=All&amp;sortField=Votes&amp;sortDirection=Descending&amp;page=0&amp;reasonClosed=All\">few fixes to Unobtrusive Ajax</a>:</p> \n<ul> \n <li><a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/1487\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/1487\">Support \"this\" context for ajax actions/forms</a> </li> \n <li><a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/238\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/238\">Unobtrusive.Ajax interferes with the cancel convention on validation</a> </li> \n <li><a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/1520\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/1520\">ASP.NET MVC 3 LoadingElementDuration is not working</a> </li> \n</ul> \n<p>I thought the first fix was pretty interesting: a <a href=\"http://stackoverflow.com/questions/6630189/how-to-use-this-inside-mvc3-ajax-actionlink-onbegin-oncomplete-events/14611314#14611314\" mce_href=\"http://stackoverflow.com/questions/6630189/how-to-use-this-inside-mvc3-ajax-actionlink-onbegin-oncomplete-events/14611314#14611314\">question came up on StackOverflow</a>, someone posted <a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/1487\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/workitem/1487\">a suggested one line fix on a CodePlex issue</a>, and it got <a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/8a2c969ab6b41591e6a7194028b5b37a562c855a\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/8a2c969ab6b41591e6a7194028b5b37a562c855a\">fixed in this commit</a>.</p> \n<p>This issue allows callbacks from Unobtrusive Ajax to have access to the initiating element.&nbsp; That's pretty handy when you've got multiple potential callers, e.g. a list of items which contain Ajax.ActionLink calls. In the past, I've had to write <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/mvc/tutorials/mvc-music-store/mvc-music-store-part-8\" mce_href=\"http://www.asp.net/mvc/tutorials/mvc-music-store/mvc-music-store-part-8\">unnecessarily complicated JavaScript to wire things up manually</a> because I couldn't take advantage of the OnBegin, OnComplete, OnFailure and OnSuccess options, e.g.</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: js; auto-links: false;\">&lt;script type=\"text/javascript\"&gt;\n    $(function () {\n        // Document.ready -&gt; link up remove event handler\n        $(\".RemoveLink\").click(function () {\n            // Get the id from the link\n            var recordToDelete = $(this).attr(\"data-id\");\n            if (recordToDelete != '') {\n                // Perform the ajax post\n                $.post(\"/ShoppingCart/RemoveFromCart\", {\"id\": recordToDelete },\n                    function (data) {\n                        // Successful requests get here\n                        // Update the page elements\n                        if (data.ItemCount == 0) {\n                            $('#row-' + data.DeleteId).fadeOut('slow');\n                        } else {\n                            $('#item-count-' + data.DeleteId).text(data.ItemCount);\n                        }\n                        $('#cart-total').text(data.CartTotal);\n                        $('#update-message').text(data.Message);\n                        $('#cart-status').text('Cart (' + data.CartCount + ')');\n                    });\n            }\n        });\n    });\n&lt;/script&gt;</pre> \n<p>Now with this change, I'd have the option of wiring up the Ajax call and success callbacks separately and tersely, since they'd have access to the calling element for the ID.</p> \n<p>That's it for this post, in the next (and definitely last) post of this series I'll look at some ASP.NET Web API 2.1 improvements.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\"> \n <a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/jongalloway?a=P7qLADb3rRg:_xuZ-vfgobM:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img 09:30:59","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:30:59","title":"Two great Kickstarter projects ending soon: Durandal JavaScript framework and bitCommander File Manager","link":"https://weblogs.asp.net:443/jongalloway/two-great-kickstarter-projects-ending-soon-durandal-javascript-framework-and-bitcommander-file-manager","description":"<p>I like Kickstarter. It's not a store, it's an easy way to help fund creative projects. You back projects you find exciting at different reward levels, and then follow along with the project's progress. I blogged about the <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2013/06/19/last-chance-to-back-the-agent-watch-kickatarter-first-chance-to-play-with-the-code-bonus-extra-credit-custom-device-emulator-with-screen-scaling.aspx\">AGENT watch last June</a> and was excited to see them hit 10x their goal - I'm really looking forward to getting my AGENT watch when they ship.</p> \n<p>Preemptive disclaimer: As I said, it's not a store. You're funding a project with goals and risks and target timelines. The average contribution amounts aren't that high and I fund projects that I want to support, so I'm okay with that. If you're not, Kickstarter's not your thing, and that's fine too.</p> \n<p>I wanted to help get the word out about two recent Kickstarter projects I've backed (and been tweeting about): <a href=\"http://www.kickstarter.com/projects/eisenbergeffect/durandal-2014\">Durandal</a> and <a href=\"http://www.kickstarter.com/projects/953554185/bitcommander\">bitCommander</a>. Both end soon (bitCommander ends on Jan 9, Durandal ends on Jan 10).</p> \n<h2>Durandal</h2> \n<p><a href=\"http://durandaljs.com/\">Durandal</a> is an open source JavaScript framework that helps you build full-featured single page applications. <a href=\"http://neverindoubtnet.blogspot.com/2014/01/hooray-for-durandal-nextgen.html\">Ward Bell summed up what's unique about Durandal pretty well</a>:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>What’s so great about it? What’s distinctive about it? <a href=\"http://www.kickstarter.com/projects/eisenbergeffect/durandal-2014/posts/703625\">Rob has his inventory</a> of laudable features (and its impressive). Here are some of the aspects of nextGen Durandal that I love … and that have no equal among alternative frameworks:</p> \n <ul> \n  <li><strong>Convention over configuration</strong> – I hate writing and maintaining “switch board” code to connect FooViewModel to FooView and FooRoute etc. I want to say “Foo” and be done with it … until and unless I have a compelling reason to break convention. </li> \n  <li><strong>Customizable conventions</strong> – Rob makes good choices but I’m free to define my own. </li> \n  <li><strong>Page life-cycle</strong> – Durandal has baked in understanding of the birth and death of “pages” so I don’t have to make up my own hacks to ensure that new pages are initialized on creation and cleaned up on destruction. </li> \n  <li><strong>Asynchrony throughout</strong> – Need to wait for the user to confirm or cancel before moving off the page? That’s easy in Durandal because asynchrony is plumbed through the page life-cycle and everywhere else. Dynamically load optional modules on-demand? Easy. </li> \n  <li><strong>Diagnostics</strong> – With debug mode turned on the console tells me exactly what choices Durandal is making for me as they happen. I can tap into that logging pipeline with my own diagnostics. </li> \n  <li><strong>Write less, do more</strong> – You all know what I mean. We all want to write less code. That’s the motherhood and apple pie that every framework promises. They usually deliver something else. Check out the <a href=\"http://vimeo.com/82601948\">nextGen Durandal sample video</a> and tell me what other technology is that clear and concise. </li> \n </ul> \n</blockquote> \n<p>We talked to Rob Eisenberg about what he wants to accomplish on the Durandal Kickstarter on Herding Code: <a href=\"http://herdingcode.com/herding-code-182-durandal-kickstarter-with-rob-eisenberg/\">Herding Code 182: Durandal Kickstarter with Rob Eisenberg</a></p> \n<p>Download / Listen:</p> \n<audio controls=\"\"> \n <source src=\"http://herdingcode.com/wp-content/uploads/HerdingCode-0182-Durandal-Kickstarter.mp3\" type=\"audio/mpeg\"> <a href=\"http://herdingcode.com/wp-content/uploads/HerdingCode-0182-Durandal-Kickstarter.mp3\">http://herdingcode.com/wp-content/uploads/HerdingCode-0182-Durandal-Kickstarter.mp3</a> </source>\n</audio>  \n<p>One of the things that really stood out for me was what Rob's doing with cutting edge browser and JavaScript technologies for the Durandal nextGen. He's investing heavily in a module system that works with ES6 modules, AMD and CommonJS. He's building on HTML Templates and Web Components. In addition to light weight, modular code, he's seeing 2-3x performance benefits over AngularJS. And it's all polyfilled so it works in current browsers while waiting for these features to ship. The result is that this is a great investment not only in a great next-generation SPA framework, but in the web in general: by backing Rob, you're getting real-world implementation of these specs early so we can get them right the first time.</p> \n<p>The main benefit of backing this Kickstarter is that you free Rob up from consulting work so Durandal gets these amazing features faster. The secondary benefit for backers is that Rob will be producing some great training series - video and source code included.</p> \n<p>As of now, there's still a good way to go on getting this funded. Pitch in and tell someone else to do so, too!</p> \n<h2>bitCommander</h2> \n<p>bitCommander is a really interesting file manager for Windows. It's already hit its funding goal as I write this. If you beat the end of the Kickstarter campaign, you can get a discount on it, and if not you'll want to pay the full price for it because this thing is looking really cool. </p> \n<p>I like the recent changes to the Windows File Explorer in Windows 8 - the ribbon's really nice, and the file copy experience is vastly improved, for instance. But when you're doing things that involve multiple simultaneous views - like assembling a lot of files, for instance - having several instances of File Explorer open gets clunky.</p> \n<p>Check out how browsing in bitCommander looks (all images below from the <a href=\"http://bit-commander.com/\">bitCommander website</a>): </p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-01-08_13h36_32\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/11843915706/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"2014-01-08_13h36_32\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5486/11843915706_d2ec789ccc_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>You might think it's just <a href=\"http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miller_Columns\">Miller columns</a> (recently popularized as the OS X Finder columns view mode, but dates all the way back to 1980). That alone would be pretty nice, but there's a lot more to it. There are tons of other great features, but I think my favorite is the temp list and batch support.</p> \n<p><img src=\"http://bit-commander.com/GIF/bitcommander-batch2.gif\" /></p> \n<p>There's a lot of other cool stuff, like tabs, nice thumbnail display (with details), intelligent filename shortening and more. I think what I like most about this is that it adds a lot of features but keep the design simple and focused. See what I mean on the <a href=\"http://bit-commander.com/\">bitCommander website</a>.</p> \n<p>Here's mockup of a possible additional feature that Milos is looking at adding: integrated command windows, sync'd to the current browsing directory:</p> \n<p><img src=\"http://bit-commander.com/Mockup-CommandLine.jpg\" /></p> \n<p>Un-disclaimer: I backed both of these projects and don't get anything for talking about them. I just like them.</p> \n<p>Go back <a href=\"http://www.kickstarter.com/projects/953554185/bitcommander\">bitCommander</a> and <a href=\"http://www.kickstarter.com/projects/eisenbergeffect/durandal-2014\">Durandal</a>!</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\"> \n <a 09:30:59","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:30:59","title":"Looking at ASP.NET MVC 5.1 and Web API 2.1 - Part 4 - Web API Help Pages, BSON, and Global Error Handling","link":"https://weblogs.asp.net:443/jongalloway/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-4-web-api-help-pages-bson-and-global-error-handling","description":"<p>This is part 4 of a series covering some of the new features in the ASP.NET MVC 5.1 and Web API 2.1 releases. The last one! If you've read them all, you have earned twelve blog readership points... after you finish this one, of course. Here are the previous posts:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Part 1: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/21/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-1-overview-and-enums.aspx\" mce_href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/21/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-1-overview-and-enums.aspx\">Overview and Enums</a> </li> \n <li>Part 2: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/24/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-2-attribute-routing-with-custom-constraints.aspx\" mce_href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/24/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-2-attribute-routing-with-custom-constraints.aspx\">Attribute Routing with Custom Constraints</a> </li> \n <li>Part 3: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/28/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-3-bootstrap-and-javascript-enhancements.aspx\">Bootstrap and JavaScript enhancements</a> </li> \n <li>Part 4: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/02/26/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-4-web-api-help-pages-bson-and-global-error-handling.aspx\">Web API Help Pages, BSON, and Global Error Handling</a></li> \n</ul> \n<p>The sample project covering the posts in this series is <a href=\"https://github.com/jongalloway/StarDotOne\">here</a>; other referenced samples are in the <a href=\"http://aspnet.codeplex.com/sourcecontrol/latest#Samples/ReadMe.txt\">ASP.NET sample repository</a>.</p> \n<p>As a reminder, Part 1 explained that ASP.NET MVC 5.1 / Web API 2.1 is a NuGet update for the MVC 5 / Web API 2 releases that shipped with Visual Studio 2013. There will be a Visual Studio update that will make them the defaults when you create new projects.</p> \n<p>In this post, we'll look at new features in ASP.NET Web API 2.1.</p> \n<h2>Attribute Routing</h2> \n<p>We already looked at the updates to Attribute Routing improvements for both ASP.NET Web API and MVC in the second post in this series, I just want to call it out again since this post is overviewing all of the other new features in ASP.NET Web API 2.1 and the Attribute Routing support for custom constraints is one of the top features in the ASP.NET Web API 2.1 release.</p> \n<p>As a reminder, custom route constraints make it really easy to create wrap route matching logic in a constraint which can then be placed on ApiControllers or actions like this:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false; highlight: [1,7];\">[VersionedRoute(\"api/Customer\", 1)]\npublic class CustomerVersion1Controller : ApiController\n{\n    // controller code goes here\n}\n[VersionedRoute(\"api/Customer\", 2)]\npublic class CustomerVersion2Controller : ApiController\n{\n    // controller code goes here\n}</pre> \n<p>In that example, the custom VersionedRoute constraint looks for an api-version header and forwards the request to the correct controller. <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/24/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-2-attribute-routing-with-custom-constraints.aspx\" mce_href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/24/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-2-attribute-routing-with-custom-constraints.aspx\">See the post for more information</a>, including a link to the sample application.</p> \n<h2>Help Page improvements</h2> \n<p>Okay, let's dig into some of the cool new features we haven't seen yet. To start with, I'm going to scaffold out a new PersonApiController using the same Person class I've used earlier in this series, shown below:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: js; auto-links: false;\">public class Person\n{\n    [ScaffoldColumn(false)]\n    public int Id { get; set; }\n    [UIHint(\"Enum-radio\")]\n    public Salutation Salutation { get; set; }\n    [Display(Name = \"First Name\")]\n    [MinLength(3, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be at least {1} characters long\")]\n    [MaxLength(100, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be no more than {1} characters\")]\n   public string FirstName { get; set; }\n    [Display(Name = \"Last Name\")]\n    [MinLength(3, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be at least {1} characters long\")]\n    [MaxLength(100, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be no more than {1} characters\")]\n    public string LastName { get; set; }\n    public int Age { get; set; }\n}\n//I guess technically these are called honorifics\npublic enum Salutation : byte\n{\n    [Display(Name = \"Mr.\")]   Mr,\n    [Display(Name = \"Mrs.\")]  Mrs,\n    [Display(Name = \"Ms.\")]   Ms,\n    [Display(Name = \"Dr.\")]   Doctor,\n    [Display(Name = \"Prof.\")] Professor,\n    Sir, \n    Lady, \n    Lord\n}</pre> \n<p>And we're using the standard Web API scaffolding:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-01-30_16h11_27\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12228468183/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12228468183/\"><img alt=\"2014-01-30_16h11_27\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7343/12228468183_ff9c844c8e.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7343/12228468183_ff9c844c8e.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Nothing has really changed for the top level ASP.NET Web API Help Page - you get a generated list of API calls for each API Controller.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-12_23h20_49\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12495094414/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12495094414/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-12_23h20_49\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3711/12495094414_6afae7eb09.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3711/12495094414_6afae7eb09.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>What has changed is what you see when you click through on one of the API calls, e.g. the PersonApi GET method. Here's how that looked in ASP.NET Web API 2.1:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-17_13h40_46\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12598874764/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12598874764/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-17_13h40_46\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2817/12598874764_9baa1098f0_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2817/12598874764_9baa1098f0_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>It shows sample data in JSON and XML, and you can kind of guess what they are if you've named your model properties well, but there's no information on type, model attributes, validation rules, etc.</p> \n<p>Here's how it looks in ASP.NET Web API 2:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-17_13h41_45\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12598899184/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12598899184/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-17_13h41_45\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2860/12598899184_be54e7720a_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2860/12598899184_be54e7720a_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>The Response Formats section hasn't changed, but now we have a Resource Description area at the top. Let's take a closer look at that:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-17_13h53_18\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12599121614/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12599121614/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-17_13h53_18\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3779/12599121614_b2cbe8590e_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3779/12599121614_b2cbe8590e_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Here we're clearly displaying both the type and validation rules.</p> \n<p>Note that the Salutation type is hyperlinked, since it's using our custom Salutation enum. Clicking through shows the possible values for that enum:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-17_13h47_37\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12598689633/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12598689633/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-17_13h47_37\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3823/12598689633_aeeaff5074_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3823/12598689633_aeeaff5074_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p><b>If you've done any work integrating with API's that had minimal or out of date documentation, hopefully the value of the above is really clear. What's great is that this is generated for me at runtime, so it's always up to date with the latest code. If my Web API is in production and I add a new Enum value or change a validation rule, the live documentation on the web site is immediately updated without any work or extra thought on my part as soon as I deploy the code.</b></p> \n<h3>Short detour: Filling in Descriptions using C# /// Comments</h3> \n<p>Now that we've got documentation for our model types, it's clear that we could improve it a bit. The most obvious thing is that there's no provided description. That's easy to add using C# /// comments (aka XML Comments). ASP.NET Web API Help Pages have had support for /// comments documentation for a while, it just hasn't been this obvious.</p> \n<p>The ASP.NET Web API Help Pages are implemented in a really clear, open model: it's all implemented in an ASP.NET MVC Area within your existing site. If you're not familiar with ASP.NET MVC Areas, they're a way to allow you to segment your applications into with separate routes, models, views and controllers but still keep them in the same project so it's easier to manage, share resources, etc.</p> \n<p>Here's the Help Page Area within the sample project we're working on:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-17_14h36_48\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12599963954/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12599963954/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-17_14h36_48\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2863/12599963954_f08e78d026_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2863/12599963954_f08e78d026_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>1. In the above screenshot, I've highlighted the \\App_Start\\HelpPageConfig.cs file because that's where we're going to set up the XML comments. There's a Register method right at the top with the following two lines:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">//// Uncomment the following to use the documentation from XML documentation file.\n//config.SetDocumentationProvider(new XmlDocumentationProvider(HttpContext.Current.Server.MapPath(\"~/App_Data/XmlDocument.xml\")));</pre> \n<p>So to use that, we'll uncomment the second line, just as the instructions say.</p> \n<p>2. Note that the comments are pointing to an XmlDocument.xml file. We need to check a box in the project settings to generate that XML file as shown below.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-17_14h46_38\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12599717125/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12599717125/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-17_14h46_38\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5537/12599717125_e0e8763f1a_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5537/12599717125_e0e8763f1a_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>That's it!</p> \n<p>Once that's done, I'm going to throw the /// comments on the controller methods and model properties and generate XML comments. I used <a href=\"http://submain.com/products/ghostdoc.aspx\" mce_href=\"http://submain.com/products/ghostdoc.aspx\">GhostDoc</a> to generate the comments, then cleaned them up and editorialized a bit.</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">/// &lt;summary&gt;\n/// This is an example person class. It artisanally crafted by a\n/// bearded, bespeckled craftsman after being lovingly sketched\n/// in a leather bound notebook with charcoal pencils.\n/// &lt;/summary&gt;\npublic class Person\n{\n    [ScaffoldColumn(false)]\n    public int Id { get; set; }\n    /// &lt;summary&gt;\n    /// This uses a custom salution enum since there's apparently no ISO standard.\n    /// &lt;/summary&gt;\n    /// &lt;value&gt;\n    /// The person's requested salutation.\n    /// &lt;/value&gt;\n    [UIHint(\"Enum-radio\")]\n    public Salutation Salutation { get; set; }\n    [Display(Name = \"First Name\")]\n    [MinLength(3, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be at least {1} characters long\")]\n    [MaxLength(100, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be no more than {1} characters\")]\n   public string FirstName { get; set; }\n    [Display(Name = \"Last Name\")]\n    [MinLength(3, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be at least {1} characters long\")]\n    [MaxLength(100, ErrorMessage = \"Your {0} must be no more than {1} characters\")]\n    public string LastName { get; set; }\n    /// &lt;summary&gt;\n    /// This is the person's actual or desired age.\n    /// &lt;/summary&gt;\n    /// &lt;value&gt;\n    /// The age in years, represented in an integer.\n    /// &lt;/value&gt;\n    public int Age { get; set; }\n}</pre> \n<p>And here's the updated help page with the descriptions:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-17_15h04_16\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12600033795/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12600033795/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-17_15h04_16\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7302/12600033795_f95a2754d6_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7302/12600033795_f95a2754d6_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>There are a ton of other features in the HelpPageConfig - you could pull your documentation from a database or CMS, for example. And since it's all implemented in standard ASP.NET MVC, you can modify the views or do whatever else you want. But it's nice to have these new features available out of the box.</p> \n<h2>BSON</h2> \n<p>BSON is a binary serialization format that's similar to JSON in that they both store name-value pairs, but it's quite different in how the data is actually stored. BSON serializes data in a binary format, which can offer some performance benefits for encode / decode / traversal.&nbsp; It's been possible to hook up a custom BSON formatter in ASP.NET Web API before; <a href=\"http://www.strathweb.com/2012/07/bson-binary-json-and-how-your-web-api-can-be-even-faster/\" mce_href=\"http://www.strathweb.com/2012/07/bson-binary-json-and-how-your-web-api-can-be-even-faster/\">Filip and others have written comprehensive blog posts describing how to do just that</a>. It's even easier now - both for clients and servers - since the BSON formatter is included with ASP.NET Web API.</p> \n<p><b>Important note:</b> BSON isn't designed to be more compact than JSON, in fact it's often bigger (depending on your data structure and content). That's because, unlike JSON, BSON embeds type information in the document. That makes for fast scan and read, but it's holding more data than the equivalent JSON document. That means that it will be faster in some cases, but it may be slower in other cases if your messages are bigger. This shows the value of content negotiation and flexible formatters in ASP.NET Web API - you can easily try out different formatters, both on the client and server side, and use the best one for the job.</p> \n<p>I'll look at two examples here.</p> \n<h3>Testing BSON with a text-heavy model</h3> \n<p>For the first example, I'll use the Person class we've been using for our previous examples. I'd like to have a lot more people in my database. I grabbed some absolutely silly code I wrote 7 years ago that generates fake surnames (<a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2007/01/12/code-puzzle-2-generate-random-fake-surnames-recap.aspx\" mce_href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2007/01/12/code-puzzle-2-generate-random-fake-surnames-recap.aspx\">Generate random fake surnames</a>) and added a controller action to slam 500 new people with a first name of Bob but a random last name and age into the database. Then I clicked on it a few times.</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-17_16h19_50\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12601399413/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12601399413/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-17_16h19_50\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7445/12601399413_a3ff443d5d.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7445/12601399413_a3ff443d5d.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Turning on the BSON formatter is just a one line code change:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false; highlight: [5];\">public static class WebApiConfig\n{\n    public static void Register(HttpConfiguration config)\n    {\n        config.Formatters.Add(new BsonMediaTypeFormatter());\n        // ...\n    }\n}</pre> \n<p>Now whenever a client sends an Accept header for application/bson, they'll get the data in BSON format. For comparison, I'm making two requests in Fiddler. Here's a request with no Accept header specified, so we get JSON:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-18_00h48_12\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12608617655/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12608617655/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-18_00h48_12\" src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2810/12608617655_5096fc8815_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm3.static.flickr.com/2810/12608617655_5096fc8815_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>The content-length there is 118,353 bytes.</p> \n<p>Now I'm setting an Accept header with application/bson:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-18_00h51_19\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12609081114/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12609081114/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-18_00h51_19\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5472/12609081114_9d2b98729c_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5472/12609081114_9d2b98729c_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Notice that <b>this BSON request is 134,395 bytes, or 13% larger</b>. I've marked some of the type identifiers in there, but you can see there are a lot more since they're lined up in columns.</p> \n<p>Place your bets: think the faster BSON serializer will be faster, despite the larger payload size? Before we answer that, we'll add in a second scenario that replaces our text-heavy Person class with a quite exciting BoringData class that's mostly numeric and binary data:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">public class BoringData\n{\n    public int Id { get; set; }\n    public long DataLong { get; set; }\n    public byte[] DataBytes { get; set; }\n    public DateTime DataDate { get; set; }\n}</pre> \n<p>And here's the test we'll throw at both of these:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">private HttpClient _client = new HttpClient();\nstatic void Main(string[] args)\n{\n    try\n    {\n        Console.WriteLine(\"Hit ENTER to begin...\");\n        RunAsync().Wait();\n    }\n    finally\n    {\n        Console.WriteLine(\"Hit ENTER to exit...\");\n        Console.ReadLine();\n    }\n}\nprivate async static Task RunAsync()\n{\n    using (HttpClient client = new HttpClient())\n    {\n        await RunTimedTest&lt;BoringData&gt;(client, new JsonMediaTypeFormatter(), \"http://localhost:29108/api/BoringDataApi\", \"application/json\");\n        await RunTimedTest&lt;BoringData&gt;(client, new BsonMediaTypeFormatter(), \"http://localhost:29108/api/BoringDataApi\", \"application/bson\");\n        await RunTimedTest&lt;Person&gt;(client, new JsonMediaTypeFormatter(), \"http://localhost:29108/api/PersonApi\", \"application/json\");\n        await RunTimedTest&lt;Person&gt;(client, new BsonMediaTypeFormatter(), \"http://localhost:29108/api/PersonApi\", \"application/bson\");\n    }\n}\npublic static async Task RunTimedTest&lt;T&gt;(HttpClient _client, MediaTypeFormatter formatter, string Uri, string mediaHeader)\n{\n    int iterations = 500;\n    _client.DefaultRequestHeaders.Accept.Clear();\n    _client.DefaultRequestHeaders.Accept.Add(new System.Net.Http.Headers.MediaTypeWithQualityHeaderValue(mediaHeader));\n    MediaTypeFormatter[] formatters = new MediaTypeFormatter[] { formatter };\n    var watch = Stopwatch.StartNew();\n    for (int i = 0; i &lt; iterations; i++)\n    {\n        var result = await _client.GetAsync(Uri);\n        var value = await result.Content.ReadAsAsync&lt;T[]&gt;(formatters);\n    }\n    Console.WriteLine(\"Format: {0,-20} Type: {1,-15}\\t Time (s):{2,10:ss\\\\.fff}\", mediaHeader, typeof(T).Name, watch.Elapsed);\n}</pre> \n<p>The BoringDataApi controller's GET method returns lots of data, as you'd expect:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">public class BoringDataApiController : ApiController\n{\n    static Random rng = new Random(Guid.NewGuid().GetHashCode());\n    public IEnumerable&lt;BoringData&gt; GetBoringData()\n    {\n        return GetLotsOfBoringData(100);\n    }\n    private IEnumerable&lt;BoringData&gt; GetLotsOfBoringData(int quantity)\n    {\n        byte[] buf1 = new byte[10000];\n        byte[] buf2 = new byte[64];\n\n        for (int i = 1; i &lt; quantity; i++)\n        {\n            rng.NextBytes(buf1);\n            rng.NextBytes(buf2);\n            yield return new BoringData\n            {\n                Id  = i,\n                DataBytes = buf1,\n                DataDate = DateTime.UtcNow,\n                DataLong = BitConverter.ToInt64(buf2,0)\n            };\n        }\n    }\n}</pre> \n<p>So, big picture, the test harness will run 500 end to end tests on both controllers, requesting both Person and BoringData as both JSON and BSON. What we're <b>not comparing</b> is the difference between the Person and BoringData responses, we're just looking for some general trends to see if BSON is faster than JSON for a mostly-textual and mostly-binary model. <a href=\"http://kellabyte.com/2014/02/12/create-benchmarks-and-results-that-have-value/\" mce_href=\"http://kellabyte.com/2014/02/12/create-benchmarks-and-results-that-have-value/\">Yes, Kelly Sommers will beat me up for this, and I'm okay with that</a>. My goal is to get some basic guidelines on when BSON works better than JSON. </p> \n<p>The real point here is that you won't know how your API or a specific content type will perform until you test it.</p> \n<p>So how'd we do?</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-02-18_17h13_33\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12624517414/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12624517414/\"><img alt=\"2014-02-18_17h13_33\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5540/12624517414_10643b7003_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5540/12624517414_10643b7003_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>In this case (and I ran this test many times with the same general result) BSON was a lot faster for mostly binary/numeric data, and a little slower for mostly textual data. In this pretty random example, <b>BSON was 140% faster for the mostly binary case and and 21% slower for the mostly-textual case. </b>That's because both serialize textual data to UTF-8, but BSON includes some additional metadata.</p> \n<p><b>So, very generally speaking, if your service returns a lot of binary / numeric / non-textual data, you should really look at BSON.</b> If you're looking for a silver bullet, you may have to pony up for some silver.</p> \n<h3>Easier implementations due to BaseJsonMediaTypeFormatter</h3> \n<p>Yes, that's the most boring heading you'll ever see. But it's hopefully true. The new BaseJsonMediaTypeFormatter has been designed to make it easier to implement new different serialization formats easier, since the the internal JSON formatters have been redesigned to make it easier to extend. I asked Doug, the dev that did most of the work for this BSON update, about his <a href=\"https://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/8b1440decc8ccc5800be799925064d5bb1aca509\" mce_href=\"https://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/8b1440decc8ccc5800be799925064d5bb1aca509\">commit message saying recent changes will make it easier to make other formatters like MessagePack happen</a> and he said:</p> \n<blockquote> \n <p>Yes.&nbsp; BaseJsonMediaTypeFormatter introduces a few Json.Net types and concepts.&nbsp; But it also provides solid and reusable async wrappers around easier-to-implement sync methods.</p> \n</blockquote> \n<p>The main thing I've noticed there is the common BaseJsonMediaTypeFormatter. There's not a whole lot of code in the <a href=\"https://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/8b1440decc8ccc5800be799925064d5bb1aca509\" mce_href=\"https://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/8b1440decc8ccc5800be799925064d5bb1aca509\">BsonMediaTypeFormatter</a>, since a lot of it's in the common base and in other support classes.</p> \n<p>And while I'm mentioning <a href=\"http://msgpack.org/\" mce_href=\"http://msgpack.org/\">MessagePack</a>, I think it's another great option that's really worth looking at, since (unlike BSON) MessagePack is designed for small message size. There's a <a href=\"https://github.com/WebApiContrib/WebAPIContrib\" mce_href=\"https://github.com/WebApiContrib/WebAPIContrib\">MsgPack formatter available now in the WebApiContrib formatters collection</a>, and Filip Woj. wrote a nice blog post overview here: <a href=\"http://www.strathweb.com/2012/09/boost-up-your-asp-net-web-api-with-messagepack/\" mce_href=\"http://www.strathweb.com/2012/09/boost-up-your-asp-net-web-api-with-messagepack/\">Boost up your ASP.NET Web API with MessagePack</a>.</p> \n<h2>Global Error Handling</h2> \n<p>The last feature we'll look at is Global Error Handling. The name's pretty self-descriptive: it lets you register handlers and loggers which will respond to any unhandled exceptions across your entire Web API application.</p> \n<p>Global error handling is especially useful in Web API because of the way the parts are so loosely coupled and composable - you've got all kinds of different handlers and filters, wired together with a very configurable system that encourages dependency injection... There's a lot going on.</p> \n<p><a title=\"ASP.NET Web API Poster\" href=\"http://www.asp.net/posters/web-api/ASP.NET-Web-API-Poster.pdf\" mce_href=\"http://www.asp.net/posters/web-api/ASP.NET-Web-API-Poster.pdf\"><img alt=\"ASP.NET Web API Poster\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3731/12758637613_45f521098d_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3731/12758637613_45f521098d_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Note: You can <a href=\"http://www.asp.net/posters/web-api/ASP.NET-Web-API-Poster.pdf\" mce_href=\"http://www.asp.net/posters/web-api/ASP.NET-Web-API-Poster.pdf\">download the PDF of this poster from the ASP.NET site</a>.</p> \n<p>That provides you tons of flexibility when you're building HTTP services, but it can make it hard to find out what's wrong when there's a problem. Exception filters help, but <a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/wikipage?title=Global%20Error%20Handling\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/wikipage?title=Global%20Error%20Handling\">as David Matson notes</a>, they don't handle:</p> \n<ol> \n <li>Exceptions thrown from controller constructors </li> \n <li>Exceptions thrown from message handlers </li> \n <li>Exceptions thrown during routing </li> \n <li>Exceptions thrown during response content serialization </li> \n</ol> \n<p>I recommend David Matson's <a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/wikipage?title=Global%20Error%20Handling\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/wikipage?title=Global%20Error%20Handling\">Web API Global Error Handling</a> wiki article entry in the ASP.NET repository for more information on design and technical implementation. The short excerpt is that you can register one IExceptionHandler and multiple IExceptionLogger instances in your application, and they'll respond to all Web API exceptions.</p> \n<p>There's already <a href=\"http://aspnet.codeplex.com/SourceControl/latest#Samples/WebApi/Elmah/ReadMe.txt\" mce_href=\"http://aspnet.codeplex.com/SourceControl/latest#Samples/WebApi/Elmah/ReadMe.txt\">a pretty clear sample in the Web API samples</a> which shows a GenericTextExceptionHandler (which returns a generic exception message for unhandled exceptions) and an ElmahExceptionLogger (which implements logging using the popular ELMAH logging system). I've been trying to come up with some other use cases, but I think they captured the main ones here - usually if you have an unhandled exception, you want to log it and make sure you return some sort of useful message to your client. </p> \n<p>Both of these interfaces are really simple - they both have a single async method (LogAsync and HandleAsync, respectively) which are passed an ExceptionContext and a CancellationToken.</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">public interface IExceptionLogger\n{\n    Task LogAsync(ExceptionLoggerContext context, CancellationToken cancellationToken);\n}\npublic interface IExceptionHandler\n{\n    Task HandleAsync(ExceptionHandlerContext context, CancellationToken cancellationToken);\n}</pre> \n<p>The ExceptionContext includes the exception as well as a lot of other useful context information:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Exception (Exception) </li> \n <li>Request (HttpRequestMessage) </li> \n <li>RequestContext () </li> \n <li>ControllerContext (HttpControllerContext) </li> \n <li>ActionContext (HttpActionContext) </li> \n <li>Response (HttpResponseMessage) </li> \n <li>CatchBlock (string) </li> \n <li>IsTopLevelCatchBlock (bool) </li> \n</ul> \n<p>They're registered in your WebApiConfig like this:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">public static class WebApiConfig\n{\n    public static void Register(HttpConfiguration config)\n    {\n        config.MapHttpAttributeRoutes();\n        // There can be multiple exception loggers. (By default, no exception loggers are registered.)\n        config.Services.Add(typeof(IExceptionLogger), new ElmahExceptionLogger());\n        // There must be exactly one exception handler. (There is a default one that may be replaced.)\n        // To make this sample easier to run in a browser, replace the default exception handler with one that sends\n        // back text/plain content for all errors.\n        config.Services.Replace(typeof(IExceptionHandler), new GenericTextExceptionHandler());\n    }\n}</pre> \n<p>There are a few areas to possibly add to, but I'm going to pass on actually implementing them so I can get this series wrapped before ASP.NET Web API 2.2 ships. Maybe an exercise for the reader?</p> \n<ul> \n <li>The sample GenericTextExceptionHandler just returns generic text, but since it has access to the exception details and the context, I'd perhaps instead return an error reference code with the response so customers of my API could use them for troubleshooting. Since one cause of API errors is an inability to access the backend database, I might instead <a href=\"https://www.runscope.com/docs/api/resources/messages#message-create\" mce_href=\"https://www.runscope.com/docs/api/resources/messages#message-create\">send an error message to Runscope</a> to give more context for debugging there as well. </li> \n <li>Rather than implement several IExceptionLogger instances, I'd probably use ELMAH's extensibility to register additional log listeners - there are listeners available for files and databases like <a href=\"http://code.google.com/p/elmah/downloads/list\" mce_href=\"http://code.google.com/p/elmah/downloads/list\">XML, JSON, SQL Server, PostgreSQL, SQLite, MySQL</a>, and lots of <a href=\"https://www.nuget.org/packages?q=elmah&amp;prerelease=&amp;sortOrder=relevance\" mce_href=\"https://www.nuget.org/packages?q=elmah&amp;prerelease=&amp;sortOrder=relevance\">NuGet packages</a> covering <a href=\"https://www.nuget.org/packages/Glimpse.Elmah/\" mce_href=\"https://www.nuget.org/packages/Glimpse.Elmah/\">Glimpse integration</a>, RavenDb, MongoDb, etc. One other idea is to <a href=\"http://docs.exceptionless.com/contents/sendingerrors/\" mce_href=\"http://docs.exceptionless.com/contents/sendingerrors/\">add in an additional logger to an additional service like Exceptionless</a>. </li> \n</ul> \n<p>Damien (damienbod) has a nice overview of Web API Exception handling, complete with a lot of references: <a href=\"http://damienbod.wordpress.com/2014/02/12/exploring-web-api-exception-handling/\" mce_href=\"http://damienbod.wordpress.com/2014/02/12/exploring-web-api-exception-handling/\">Exploring Web API Exception Handling</a></p> \n<h2>More features to read about</h2> \n<p>We've looked at several of the top features in this release, but there are a lot more. Here's a list with links to the documentation:</p> \n<p><b>ASP.NET MVC 5.1</b></p> \n<ul> \n <li><a href=\"http://www.asp.net/mvc/overview/releases/mvc51-release-notes#AttributeRouting\" repository</a>.</p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\"> \n <a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/jongalloway?a=-SguCKE2ajg:-rfz58149Q0:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img 09:30:59","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:30:59","title":"Looking at ASP.NET MVC 5.1 and Web API 2.1 - Part 2 - Attribute Routing with Custom Constraints","link":"https://weblogs.asp.net:443/jongalloway/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-2-attribute-routing-with-custom-constraints","description":"<p>I'm continuing a series looking at some of the new features in ASP.NET MVC 5.1 and Web API 2.1. Part 1 (<a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/21/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-1-overview-and-enums.aspx\" mce_href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/21/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-1-overview-and-enums.aspx\">Overview and Enums</a>) explained how to update your NuGet packages in an ASP.NET MVC application, so I won't rehash that here.</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Part 1: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/21/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-1-overview-and-enums.aspx\" mce_href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/21/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-1-overview-and-enums.aspx\">Overview and Enums</a> </li> \n <li>Part 2: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/24/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-2-attribute-routing-with-custom-constraints.aspx\" mce_href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/24/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-2-attribute-routing-with-custom-constraints.aspx\">Attribute Routing with Custom Constraints</a> </li> \n <li>Part 3: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/01/28/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-3-bootstrap-and-javascript-enhancements.aspx\">Bootstrap and JavaScript enhancements</a> </li> \n <li>Part 4: <a href=\"http://weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/archive/2014/02/26/looking-at-asp-net-mvc-5-1-and-web-api-2-1-part-4-web-api-help-pages-bson-and-global-error-handling.aspx\">Web API Help Pages, BSON, and Global Error Handling</a></li> \n</ul> \n<p>The sample project covering the posts in this series is <a href=\"https://github.com/jongalloway/StarDotOne\">here</a>; other referenced samples are in the <a href=\"http://aspnet.codeplex.com/sourcecontrol/latest#Samples/ReadMe.txt\">ASP.NET sample repository</a>.</p> \n<p>In this post, we'll look at improvements to attribute routing for both ASP.NET MVC and ASP.NET Web API. First, a quick review of what routing constraints are used for.</p> \n<h2>Intro to Routing Constraints</h2> \n<p>ASP.NET MVC and Web API have both offered both simple and custom route constraints since they first came out. A simple constraint would be something like this:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false; highlight: [3];\">routes.MapRoute(\"blog\", \"{year}/{month}/{day}\",\n    new { controller = \"blog\", action = \"index\" },\n    new { year = @\"\\d{4}\", month = @\"\\d{2}\", day = @\"\\d{2}\" });</pre> \n<p>In the above case, \"/2014/01/01\" would match but \"/does/this/work\" would not since the values don't match the required pattern.&nbsp; If you needed something more complex than a simple pattern match, you'd use a custom constraint by implementing IRouteConstraint and defining the custom logic in the Match method - if it returns true, the route is a match.</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">public interface IRouteConstraint\n{\n    bool Match(HttpContextBase httpContext, Route route, string parameterName, RouteValueDictionary values, RouteDirection routeDirection);\n}</pre> \n<h2>Route Constraints in Attribute Routing</h2> \n<p>One of the top new features in ASP.NET MVC 5 and Web API 2 was the addition of Attribute Routing. Rather than defining all your routes in /App_Start/RouteConfig.cs using a series of routes.MapRoute() calls, you can define routes using attributes on your controller actions and controller classes. You can take your pick of whichever works better to you: continue to use traditional routing, use attribute routing instead, or use them both.</p> \n<p>Attribute routing previously offered custom inline constraints, like this:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">[Route(\"temp/{scale:values(celsius|fahrenheit)}\")]</pre> \n<p>Here, the scale segment has a custom inline Values constraint which will only match if the the scale value is in the pipe-delimited list, e.g. this will match temp/celsius and /temp/fahrenheit but not /temp/foo. You can read more about the Attribute Routing features that shipped with ASP.NET MVC 5, including inline constraints like the above, on this post by Ken Egozi: <a href=\"http://blogs.msdn.com/b/webdev/archive/2013/10/17/attribute-routing-in-asp-net-mvc-5.aspx\" mce_href=\"http://blogs.msdn.com/b/webdev/archive/2013/10/17/attribute-routing-in-asp-net-mvc-5.aspx\">Attribute Routing in ASP.NET MVC 5</a>. </p> \n<p>While inline constraints allow you to restrict values for a particular segment, they're both a little limited (e.g. they can't operate over the entire URL, and some more complex thing that aren't possible at that scope). To see more about what changed and why, see the <a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/f291ab8258da6536eb74930222a9a73989128b3b\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/f291ab8258da6536eb74930222a9a73989128b3b\">issue report</a> and <a href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/f291ab8258da6536eb74930222a9a73989128b3b\" mce_href=\"http://aspnetwebstack.codeplex.com/SourceControl/changeset/f291ab8258da6536eb74930222a9a73989128b3b\">changed code for this commit</a>.</p> \n<p>Now with ASP.NET MVC 5.1, we can create a new attribute that implements a custom route constraint. Here's an example.</p> \n<h2>ASP.NET MVC 5.1 Example: Adding a custom LocaleRoute</h2> \n<p>Here's a simple custom route attribute that matches based on a list of supported locales.</p> \n<p>First, we'll create a custom LocaleRouteConstraint that implements IRouteConstraint:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">public class LocaleRouteConstraint : IRouteConstraint\n{\n\tpublic string Locale { get; private set; }\n\tpublic LocaleRouteConstraint(string locale)\n\t{\n\t\tLocale = locale;\n\t}\n\tpublic bool Match(HttpContextBase httpContext, Route route, string parameterName, RouteValueDictionary values, RouteDirection routeDirection)\n\t{\n\t\tobject value;\n\t\tif (values.TryGetValue(\"locale\", out value) &amp;&amp; !string.IsNullOrWhiteSpace(value as string))\n\t\t{\n\t\t\tstring locale = value as string;\n\t\t\tif (isValid(locale))\n\t\t\t{\n\t\t\t\treturn string.Equals(Locale, locale, StringComparison.OrdinalIgnoreCase);\n\t\t\t}\n\t\t}\n\t\treturn false;\n\t}\n\tprivate bool isValid(string locale)\n\t{\n\t\tstring[] validOptions = \"EN-US|EN-GB|FR-FR\".Split('|') ;\n\t\treturn validOptions.Contains(locale.ToUpper());\n\t}\n}</pre> \n<p>IRouteConstraint has one method, Match. That's where you write your custom logic which determines if a set of incoming route values, context, etc., match your custom route. If you return true, routes with this constraint are eligible to respond to the request; if you return false the request will not be mapped to routes with this constraint.</p> \n<p>In this case, we've got a simple isValid matcher which takes a locale string (e.g. fr-fr) and validates it against a list of supported locales. In more advanced use, this may be querying against a database backed cache of locales your site supports or using some other more advanced method. If you are working with a more advanced constraint, especially a locale constraint, I recommend Ben Foster's article <a title=\"http://ben.onfabrik.com/posts/improving-aspnet-mvc-routing-configuration\" href=\"http://ben.onfabrik.com/posts/improving-aspnet-mvc-routing-configuration\" mce_href=\"http://ben.onfabrik.com/posts/improving-aspnet-mvc-routing-configuration\">Improving ASP.NET MVC Routing Configuration</a>. </p> \n<p>It's important to see that the real value in this case is running more advanced logic than a simple pattern match - if that's all you're doing, you could use a regex inline route constraint (e.g. {x:regex(^\\d{3}-\\d{3}-\\d{4}$)}).</p> \n<p>Now we've got a constraint, but we need to map it to an attribute to use in attribute routing. Note that separating constraints from attributes gives a lot more flexibility - for instance, we could use this constraint on multiple attributes.</p> \n<p>Here's a simple one:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">public class LocaleRouteAttribute : RouteFactoryAttribute\n{\n\tpublic LocaleRouteAttribute(string template, string locale)\n\t\t: base(template)\n\t{\n\t\tLocale = locale;\n\t}\n\tpublic string Locale\n\t{\n\t\tget;\n\t\tprivate set;\n\t}\n\tpublic override RouteValueDictionary Constraints\n\t{\n\t\tget\n\t\t{\n\t\t\tvar constraints = new RouteValueDictionary();\n\t\t\tconstraints.Add(\"locale\", new LocaleRouteConstraint(Locale));\n\t\t\treturn constraints;\n\t\t}\n\t}\n\tpublic override RouteValueDictionary Defaults\n\t{\n\t\tget\n\t\t{\n\t\t\tvar defaults = new RouteValueDictionary();\n\t\t\tdefaults.Add(\"locale\", \"en-us\");\n\t\t\treturn defaults;\n\t\t}\n\t}\n}</pre> \n<p>Now we've got a complete route attribute we can place on a controller or action:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false; highlight: [5];\">using System.Web.Mvc;\nnamespace StarDotOne.Controllers\n{\n    [LocaleRoute(\"hello/{locale}/{action=Index}\", \"EN-GB\")]\n    public class ENGBHomeController : Controller\n    {\n        // GET: /hello/en-gb/\n        public ActionResult Index()\n        {\n            return Content(\"I am the EN-GB controller.\");\n        }\n    }\n}</pre> \n<p>And here's our FR-FR controller:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">using System.Web.Mvc;\nnamespace StarDotOne.Controllers\n{\n    [LocaleRoute(\"hello/{locale}/{action=Index}\", \"FR-FR\")]\n    public class FRFRHomeController : Controller\n    {\n        // GET: /hello/fr-fr/\n        public ActionResult Index()\n        {\n            return Content(\"Je suis le contrôleur FR-FR.\");\n        }\n    }\n}</pre> \n<p>Before running this, we need to verify that we've got Attribute Routes enabled in our RouteConfig:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false; highlight: [7];\">public class RouteConfig\n{\n    public static void RegisterRoutes(RouteCollection routes)\n    {\n        routes.IgnoreRoute(\"{resource}.axd/{*pathInfo}\");\n        routes.MapMvcAttributeRoutes();\n        routes.MapRoute(\n            name: \"Default\",\n            url: \"{controller}/{action}/{id}\",\n            defaults: new { controller = \"Home\", action = \"Index\", id = UrlParameter.Optional }\n        );\n    }\n}</pre> \n<p>Now a request to /hello/en-gb/ goes to our ENGBController and a request to /hello/fr-fr/ goes to the FRFRController:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-01-24_11h18_03\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12122081323/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12122081323/\"><img alt=\"2014-01-24_11h18_03\" src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5532/12122081323_30f4b1cd6f_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm6.static.flickr.com/5532/12122081323_30f4b1cd6f_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Because we've set the default locale in the LocaleRouteAttribute to en-us, we can browse to it using either /hello/en-us/ or just /hello:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-01-24_11h20_05\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12122479736/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12122479736/\"><img alt=\"2014-01-24_11h20_05\" src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3704/12122479736_545c6a0708_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm4.static.flickr.com/3704/12122479736_545c6a0708_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>If you've been paying close attention, you may be thinking that we could have accomplished the same thing using an inline route constraint. I think the real benefit over a custom inline constraint is when you're doing more than operating on one segment in the URL: preforming logic on the entire route or context. One great example there would be using a custom attribute based on a user's locale selection (set in a cookie, perhaps) or using a header.</p> \n<p>So, to recap:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>You could write custom route constraints before in \"Traditional\" code-based routing, but not in attribute routing </li> \n <li>You could write custom inline constraints, but they mapped just to a segment in the URL </li> \n <li>Custom route constraints now can operate at a higher level than just a segment on the URL path, e.g. headers or other request context </li> \n</ul> \n<p>A very common use case for using headers in routing is versioning by header. We'll look at that with ASP.NET Web API 2.1 next. Keep in mind that, while the general recommendation is to use ASP.NET Web API for your HTTP APIs, many APIs are still running on ASP.NET MVC for a variety of reasons (existing / legacy systems APIs built on ASP.NET MVC, familiarity with MVC, mostly-MVC applications with relatively few APIs that want to stay simple, developer preferences, etc.) and for that reason, versioning ASP.NET MVC HTTP APIs by headers is probably one of the top use cases of custom route attribute constaints for ASP.NET MVC as well.</p> \n<h2>ASP.NET Web API 2.1 Custom Route Attributes example: Versioning By Header</h2> \n<p>Note: The example I'm showing here is in <a href=\"http://aspnet.codeplex.com/SourceControl/latest#Samples/WebApi/RoutingConstraintsSample/ReadMe.txt\" mce_href=\"http://aspnet.codeplex.com/SourceControl/latest#Samples/WebApi/RoutingConstraintsSample/ReadMe.txt\">the official samples list on CodePlex</a>. There's a lot of great examples there, including some samples showing off some of the more complex features you don't hear about all that often. Since the methodology is almost exactly the same as what we looked at in ASP.NET MVC 5.1 and <a href=\"http://aspnet.codeplex.com/SourceControl/latest#Samples/WebApi/RoutingConstraintsSample/ReadMe.txt\" mce_href=\"http://aspnet.codeplex.com/SourceControl/latest#Samples/WebApi/RoutingConstraintsSample/ReadMe.txt\">the sample's available</a>, I'll go through this one a lot faster.</p> \n<p>First, the custom constraint:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">internal class VersionConstraint : IHttpRouteConstraint\n{\n    public const string VersionHeaderName = \"api-version\";\n    private const int DefaultVersion = 1;\n    public VersionConstraint(int allowedVersion)\n    {\n        AllowedVersion = allowedVersion;\n    }\n    public int AllowedVersion\n    {\n        get;\n        private set;\n    }\n    public bool Match(HttpRequestMessage request, IHttpRoute route, string parameterName, IDictionary&lt;string, object&gt; values, HttpRouteDirection routeDirection)\n    {\n        if (routeDirection == HttpRouteDirection.UriResolution)\n        {\n            int version = GetVersionHeader(request) ?? DefaultVersion;\n            if (version == AllowedVersion)\n            {\n                return true;\n            }\n        }\n        return false;\n    }\n    private int? GetVersionHeader(HttpRequestMessage request)\n    {\n        string versionAsString;\n        IEnumerable&lt;string&gt; headerValues;\n        if (request.Headers.TryGetValues(VersionHeaderName, out headerValues) &amp;&amp; headerValues.Count() == 1)\n        {\n            versionAsString = headerValues.First();\n        }\n        else\n        {\n            return null;\n        }\n        int version;\n        if (versionAsString != null &amp;&amp; Int32.TryParse(versionAsString, out version))\n        {\n            return version;\n        }\n        return null;\n    }\n}</pre> \n<p>This is similar to the simpler LocaleConstraint we looked at earlier, but parses an integer version number from a header. Now, like before, we create an attribute to put this constraint to work:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false;\">    internal class VersionedRoute : RouteFactoryAttribute\n    {\n        public VersionedRoute(string template, int allowedVersion)\n            : base(template)\n        {\n            AllowedVersion = allowedVersion;\n        }\n        public int AllowedVersion\n        {\n            get;\n            private set;\n        }\n        public override IDictionary&lt;string, object&gt; Constraints\n        {\n            get\n            {\n                var constraints = new HttpRouteValueDictionary();\n                constraints.Add(\"version\", new VersionConstraint(AllowedVersion));\n                return constraints;\n            }\n        }\n    }\n}</pre> \n<p>And with that set up, we can just slap the attribute header on a couple different ApiControllers:</p> \n<pre class=\"brush: csharp; auto-links: false; highlight: [1,7];\">[VersionedRoute(\"api/Customer\", 1)]\npublic class CustomerVersion1Controller : ApiController\n{\n    // controller code goes here\n}\n[VersionedRoute(\"api/Customer\", 2)]\npublic class CustomerVersion2Controller : ApiController\n{\n    // controller code goes here\n}</pre> \n<p>That's it - now requests to /api/Customer with the api-version header set to 1 (or empty, since it's the default) go to the first controller, and with api-version set to 2 go to the second controller. The sample includes a handy test client console app that does just that:</p> \n<p><a title=\"2014-01-24_14h58_06\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12124449735/\" mce_href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/12124449735/\"><img alt=\"2014-01-24_14h58_06\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7310/12124449735_156c2c6a3e_b.jpg\" border=\"0\" mce_src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7310/12124449735_156c2c6a3e_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>Okay, let's wrap up there for now. In the next (probably final) post, we'll take a quick high level look at some of the other features in this release.</p> \n<p>Recap:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Custom route constraints let you run custom logic to determine if a route matches as well as other things like compute values that are available in the matching controllers </li> \n <li>The previous release allowed for custom inline route constraints, but they only operated on a segment </li> \n <li>This *.1 release includes support for full custom route constraints </li> \n</ul>\n<div class=\"feedflare\"> \n <a No cost or obligations, and the account bookmarks where you're at across multiple courses which is pretty helpful.</em></p> \n<h2>Introduction to ASP.NET MVC</h2> \n<p>Christopher and I put on <a href=\"http://www.microsoftvirtualacademy.com/training-courses/developing-asp-net-mvc-4-web-applications-jump-start\">Developing ASP.NET MVC 4 Web Applications Jump Start</a> last year, built from the ASP.NET MVC certification course materials. That means your sites automatically adapt to look nice on desktop, laptop, tablets and phones without any hacky code (on server or client) to try to guess if the user probably wants a \"mobile\" view of the site (what does that even mean now that we've got giant phones, tiny tablets, and convertibles like the Surface?). </p> \n<p>All the ASP.NET project in Visual Studio 2013 (Web Forms and MVC) are built with Bootstrap. It's automatically in every new ASP.NET application.</p> \n<p>That's a good start, but I felt like all of the presentations and training I'd seen using on Bootstrap with ASP.NET had been pretty simple: \"Hey, check it out, we have Bootstrap now, responsive design is great, here's a new theme, yay! Done!\" That's definitely about all I'd covered in my presentations. </p> \n<p>Sure, Bootstrap is neat, but there are some challenges: The grid system can be a little complex, especially if you want your content to lay out differently at different sizes. Do I really have to memorize all those Bootstrap class names (especially fun for complex components or layouts)? How do I keep my site from looking like every other Bootstrap site out there? And my personal pet peeve: how do I take advantage of Bootstrap without turning my HTML into a horrible mess of &lt;div class=\"col-lg-6 col-md-4 col-sm-3\"&gt; Bootstrap classes that seem to want to take over?</p> \n<p>That's what this class is about. Christopher and I cover the basics pretty quickly, then go deep with things like responsive page design, making Visual Studio pick up some of the hard work, and taking advantage of Less to get the HTML under control. Here's the outline:</p> \n<ul> \n <li>Introduction to Bootstrap </li> \n <li>Bootstrap Components </li> \n <li>Page Design </li> \n <li>Visual Studio and ASP.NET Integration </li> \n <li>JavaScript Functions </li> \n <li>Using Bootstrap with LESS </li> \n <li>Bootstrap in the Real World </li>\n</ul>\n<p><br /></p>\n<ul></ul>\n<p class=\"alert alert-info\">View the course here: <a href=\"http://www.microsoftvirtualacademy.com/training-courses/building-responsive-ui-with-bootstrap\">Building Responsive UI with Bootstrap</a></p> \n<p>I'm really happy with this course - I think we really nailed it, and judging from the live response it sounds like they thought so, too. I'll do a follow-up blog post digging into the Bootstrap / Less stuff, it's pretty slick.</p> \n<p><img src=\"http://c4.staticflickr.com/4/3913/15202400432_531097d0f8_b.jpg\" /></p> \n<h2>We'll Do It Live!</h2> \n<p>As I mentioned earlier, having two trainers gives us the opportunity to do a lot of things live.
