src=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/jongalloway/~4/P2hXvLwO4PU\" height=\"1\" width=\"1\" alt=\"\" />","descriptionType":"text/html","publishedDate":"Wed, 10 Sep 2014 23:22:32 +0000","feedId":11586,"bgimg":"http://c2.staticflickr.com/6/5568/15179845646_c202c6b118_h.jpg","linkMd5":"45c5dd5a69dbfeec1cf61ffd63e6d0a0","bgimgJsdelivr":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn61@2020_6/2020/08/25/01-31-00-659_47ecc1c6f15aa414.webp","destWidth":1600,"destHeight":967,"sourceBytes":161980,"destBytes":78614,"author":"","articleImgCdnMap":{"http://c2.staticflickr.com/6/5568/15179845646_c202c6b118_h.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx32/cdn61@2020_6/2020/08/25/01-31-00-659_47ecc1c6f15aa414.webp","http://c4.staticflickr.com/4/3913/15202400432_531097d0f8_b.jpg":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx27/cdn35@2020_6/2020/08/25/01-31-05-533_7474db27b573c4df.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/jongalloway?d=yIl2AUoC8zA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx3/cdn22@2020_5/2020/08/25/01-31-00-446_8d8cac3930905542.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/jongalloway?i=P2hXvLwO4PU:oeyaoAbPWMs:V_sGLiPBpWU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx13/cdn67@2020_5/2020/08/25/01-31-05-463_f38ca9b0920abd03.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/jongalloway?d=7Q72WNTAKBA":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx14/cdn60@2020_5/2020/08/25/01-31-02-317_e42c5d9ab3613df8.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/jongalloway?d=G79ilh31hkQ":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx30/cdn3@2020_5/2020/08/25/01-31-02-342_9587137edd441c1a.webp","http://feeds.feedburner.com/~r/jongalloway/~4/P2hXvLwO4PU":"https://cdn.jsdelivr.net/gh/myreaderx10/cdn52@2020_6/2020/08/25/01-31-03-716_109b6e205735ed16.webp"},"publishedOrCreatedDate":1598319059578},{"createdTime":"2020-08-25 09:30:59","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:30:59","title":"Lessons Learned Preparing and Presenting Tech Training Events","link":"https://weblogs.asp.net:443/jongalloway/lessons-learned-preparing-and-presenting-tech-training-events","description":"<p>You've agreed to present a full day training event, and the day's approaching. What do you do?</p> \n<p>I'm not a training expert, but I've learned a few lessons on the job over the past few years that I think will help. I'd done some technical presentations over the years, but in the 4+ years I've been at Microsoft I've been doing quite a bit more. I've presented at dozens of Web Camp events (both one and two day) all over the world, been in charge of the Web Camps Training Kit for the past few years, helped plan and present two <a href=\"http://www.microsoftvirtualacademy.com/training-courses/developing-asp-net-mvc-4-web-applications-jump-start\">Microsoft Virtual Academy events</a> (both full day, <a href=\"http://channel9.msdn.com/Series/Building-Web-Apps-with-ASP-NET-Jump-Start\">almost 400k online views for some of the sessions</a>). I've also presented some smaller training workshops to some development teams at some large companies as they were looking at making some technology transitions.</p> \n<p>Most of these events went pretty well. Some of them, towards the beginning and despite my best efforts, did not go well. Here's what I've learned so far.</p> \n<h2>Different From One Hour Tech Presentation</h2> \n<p>I'm going to start with the planning stages in a second, but we need to get one thing out of the way first. A training event is significantly different from a one hour technical presentation. Many of the things are similar - you can start to get a handle on it by breaking an eight hour day into a series of eight one hour talks - but it's different enough that some things you can get away with for an hour won't work for eight, and vice versa.</p> \n<p>Just a few of the important differences:</p> \n<ol> \n <li>Expectations for the balance training vs. entertaining are different. When people invest a day attending training, they have higher expectations of learning something, whereas some attendees of a one hour session - especially at a conference or code camp - are just \"channel surfing\" and have greater expectations for entertainment. On the other hand, it's perhaps more important to strategically entertain / engage during a full day of training to break it up. </li> \n <li>A full day of training is exhausting. I know an hour presentation seems tiring, but wait until you've done a full day. It's a marathon, not a sprint. </li> \n <li>Organization and timing are more important. You might be able to get away with rambling and poor time management for an hour, but it won't fly when you're doing multiple sessions. </li> \n</ol> \n<p>Note: Keep in mind that this post is focused on presenting several consecutive hours of technical training. Many of the recommendations I'll give will also apply to your standard one hour technical presentation, but keep in mind that I'm not writing about How To Give A Technical Presentation. </p> \n<h2>Planning</h2> \n<h3>Ask Questions Early</h3> \n<p>If at possible, do a short pre-meeting before you get too much into planning. I generally hate meetings and avoid them when possible. This is a meeting you want to have. I get different answers in a meeting than I do in an e-mail thread. </p> \n<p>Find out the following:</p> \n<ol> \n <li>Who will you be speaking to (numbers, skill level, etc.)? </li> \n <li>What do they want to get out of the day? </li> \n <li>Get a list of specific questions and topics that they want to learn about. </li> \n <li>What is the expected format? Is this presentation style (attendees passively watching speaker), workshop (attendees following along on laptops), a mix, or something else? </li> \n <li>Will the presentation be filmed? It usually can be if you ask early enough. </li> \n <li>What's the tech setup? What resolution will you be projecting at? What video connector? </li> \n</ol> \n<h3>Some Anecdotes on Planning</h3> \n<p><strong>Planning win:</strong> A few years back I got a request for a day of advanced MVC training, but during a pre-meeting it came out that the team had no MVC experience and while they were experienced developers, what they needed was an introduction to MVC with a few additional, specific questions addressed at the end of the day after they were up to speed. </p> \n<p><strong>Lesson: Ask questions before (or at the very least at the beginning of) the event to determine skill and experience level, don't assume.</strong></p> \n<p><strong>Planning fail:</strong> Probably my worst presentation ever was an ASP.NET MVC bootcamp I presented at a conference. Having spent a portion of my youth in the military, I'd assumed that the participants at a bootcamp would show up expecting to do some work. In what kind of bizarro world do bootcamp attendees just kick back and watch a presentation? None, right? Right. </p> \n<p>So I prepared a long workshop style tutorial wherein I would guide attendees through building an application, completely revolving around them typing things into their laptops. I had a bunch of downloads prepared for them including checkpoints at the end of each section, etc. When I began the presentation, it quickly became apparent that none of my assumptions were correct. Many of the attendees didn't have laptops, many that did have laptops didn't have the development prerequisites, and everyone was expecting to watch me talk to them for half a day. It was horrible. Eventually I just had to give up on my plans, throw out my slides, and come up with something new on the fly. It wasn't good, but it was better than the train wreck my original lesson plan had become. Some left, some stuck around and I did my best to adapt, get those who wanted to follow along set up and speak to the rest of them. Years later, this still haunts me. I'm really sorry. </p> \n<p><strong>Lesson: Ask an organizer to make sure you're clear on the format. If you expect people to participate, make sure that's clearly communicated to them before the event. Assume people will be participating as little as they can get away with unless.</strong></p> \n<h2>Get Another Presenter</h2> \n<p>Training is SO much easier if you have another presenter who can cover a few of your talks. You'll need breathers, and you'll appreciate time to review notes and set up your demos. Attendees will appreciate the break from watching one person all day, too.</p> \n<p>This goes double if you're travelling to another time zone. It's really hard to keep focused and energetic when you're jetlagged. Breaks are important, and you'll be kept busy during scheduled breaks.</p> \n<h2>Preparing The Lesson Plan</h2> \n<h3>What Matters: The End Result</h3> \n<p>Decide what you want them to get out of the day and let that drive your outline – not the feature list or demos you want to show off, etc. What will they remember a day after your talk? Focus on that.</p> \n<p>Expectations for the balance of training vs. entertaining are different compared to a one hour presentation. When people invest a day attending training, they have higher expectations of learning something, whereas some attendees of a one hour session - especially at a conference or code camp - are just \"channel surfing\" and have greater expectations for entertainment. On the other hand, it's perhaps more important to strategically entertain / engage during a full day of training to break it up.</p> \n<p>A lot of the value you're providing in a full day of training is in the higher level conceptual models and organization. You're not just working through the feature list, you're explaining why the features exist and how they work together.</p> \n<h3>Work From An Outline</h3> \n<p>As I mentioned earlier, an eight hour training course is just eight one hour presentations. It takes time an preparation, but you can do this. Let's look at an example breakdown an eight hour course on anything - could be on How To Make Ramen Burgers:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><strong>Keynote</strong>. You always need an introduction. Explain what you'll cover, set expectations, explain the high level roadmap for the day, cover prerequisites. </li> \n <li><strong>The Tools</strong> - Explain what you'll use, how it works, where you can learn more. (note: If you're thinking this doesn't apply, remember to adapt tools to the concept. e.g. for Node, this is stuff like NPM.) </li> \n <li><strong>The Concepts</strong> - Now that we're familiar with the tools, let's build something simple and explain every concept, ever step of the way. Remind advanced folks that we're going to speed up in a bit. Let them feel superior for being so experienced (and stay awake) by asking questions. Key point: the experienced people are not your adversaries! They are your allies, and a huge asset in teaching the class. Include them, don't battle them. </li> \n <li>Deep Dive 1 </li> \n <li>Deep Dive 2 </li> \n <li>Deep Dive 3 </li> \n <li>Deep Dive 4 </li> \n <li><strong>Wrap Up.</strong> Summarize what was covered, encourage everyone with what they've learned, give resources for going further. This is important for two reasons. First, you want to tie things up conceptually so they don't just leave with \"Whoa, that was a lot of stuff. What was the middle thing?\" feeling. Second, you want to make sure they can take advantage of what you just taught them, and learn more or get help. </li> \n</ol> \n<p>Yes, steps 4 - 7 are not filled in. </p> \n<p><a title=\"sidney-harris-cartoon-a-miracle-occurs-here[1]\" href=\"http://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/13900085900/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"sidney-harris-cartoon-a-miracle-occurs-here[1]\" src=\"http://farm8.static.flickr.com/7061/13900085900_8cac31e8df_b.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>But already you're down from 8 hours to 4 hours, and that's quite something.</p> \n<p>Now, for steps 4 - 7, here's the idea. You are the expert. You've been asked to speak because you know something. You're really quite smart, and experienced. Right? If not, well... oh, dear, we should have covered that earlier. You'll really need to be somewhat experienced to do some training. If you're not, and you can't get out of this, hack like mad for a week or two to try to build some experience. Good luck!</p> \n<p>So, you're experienced. What would you tell a friend who wanted to learn about this concept? Can you break it into four big things they should know? Either four main concepts, or a four step process? Great. From there, it's just four one hour presentations.</p> \n<p>Here's what I currently cover at Web Camps:</p> \n<ol> \n <li>Keynote </li> \n <li>Introduction to ASP.NET and Visual Studio 2013 Web Tooling <em>&lt;-- The tools </em></li> \n <li>Building Web Applications using the latest ASP.NET technologies <em>&lt;-- The concepts </em></li> \n <li>Building web front ends using the latest web standards <em>&lt;-- Deep dive #1</em> </li> \n <li>API Services for both web and devices <em>&lt;-- Deep dive #2</em> </li> \n <li>Running, improving and maintaining a site in the real world <em>&lt;-- Deep dive #3</em> </li> \n <li>Real-time Communications with SignalR <em>&lt;-- Deep Dive #3 </em></li> \n <li>Wrap Up </li> \n</ol> \n<p><strong>Note:</strong> Presenting several presentations works best if there's a natural flow. In the above case, it's something like <em>Create -&gt; Build -&gt; Deploy</em>. </p> \n<p>But be willing to make exceptions. For this example, you'll see there's one small thing that's out of order: Sessions 6 is post-deployment, and 7 is a dev topic. That is completely intentional, based on presenting this content a few times. <a href=\"http://asp.net/signalr\">SignalR</a> is cool and exciting. It's a highlight, and it's fun to end on. Scaling and maintaining a site in production is really important, but it's hard work and requires more energy than most attendees will have at the end of the day. So I break my logical flow to put the hawt guitar solo at the end. And, honestly, to keep people from leaving after session 5, or at lunch time.</p> \n<h3>A Theme</h3> \n<p>You've been to technical training, right? It gets hard to keep perspective and focus after a few hours. One really useful way to simplify that is to have a common theme. For me, that's usually a common scenario: we're building a site that does X. It doesn't really matter what X is as long as it fits all of your scenarios and you can excited about it. Really, it should be something you can get excited about. Do you like golfing? Make a golf tracker site, not a Contoso Widget Inventory System. And definitely not a Foo app that tracks Bar and Baz instances.</p> \n<ol>\n <!--EndFragment-->\n</ol> \n<h2>Demos</h2> \n<p>Okay, let's start with the bad news:</p> \n<ol> \n <li>The only way to keep people awake and interested in a full day training course is with a lot of demos. </li> \n <li>It is exponentially harder to do a full day of demos than it is to do a single hour of demos. The hardest part is that you don't really get any set up time in between sessions, but even if you do it might get monopolized by someone with an obscure question who isn't thinking about the time it takes to set up demos for the next session. </li> \n</ol> \n<p>So, just plan to give 8 presentations without any time in between them. (Note: Remember that I said it's great to have another presenter? Yeah. Any time another presenter speaks, it resets your clock. Gold.)</p> \n<h3>Begin / End States</h3> \n<p>Okay, we've built up some stress. You have to give eight presentations, you can't count on having any prep time between them, and they have to be good. What can you do?</p> \n<p>Here's where prep can really help.&nbsp; The idea is that, for each demo, you have a begin state (where the demo starts) and an end state (the way the demo should look when your demo is done). This really helps with your peace of mind: for every demo, you've got a set place to start and - more importantly - a working demo to fall back to in case everything goes wrong. Just like washing your car ensures it won't rain, having a working (and validated) end state for each demo does wonders for both the demo and your peace of mind. Trust me. And, if you get nervous (like me) it's so great knowing there's a working example of your demo app in case things just won't cooperate.</p> \n<p>You can do this using source control labels if those come naturally to you. For the Web Camps Training Kit, we just have subfolders with begin and end stats.</p> \n<h3>Strategic Typing</h3> \n<p>Think about what you'll be live coding. Does this add value? Does it teach people? Are you 100% certain you can pull it off without dealing with a&nbsp; minor typo here or there? Hey, if you're a ninja coder, more power to you. Run that kata.</p> \n<p>For the rest of us, think of what you'll actually show, and what your attendees will profit from seeing. Forget about impressing them, focus on the actual value of having 100 or so people watch you type. As I've done this, I've moved to showing a lot of my demos as tours rather than \"feats of strength\" - here's what the code looks like, let's talk about it.</p> \n<p>I realize I'm not going to impress people with this technique, or get high speaker ratings, or any of that. I'm okay with it. I have a limited amount of time, I choose to focus it on making my viewers successful rather than trying to make me successful.</p> \n<p>But, recognize that just watching someone explain code can be boring, too. So I try to pick strategic bits and pieces. An easy way to interact with the code is to intentionally break it, or change output text.</p> \n<h3>Reset Scripts</h3> \n<p>Doubling back a bit here, but if you're going to give the same training several times, what do you do? The simplest solution is to have one source&nbsp; of truth, and you copy from that into a presentation directory each time. That totally works. The important bit is not to present from the source directory.</p> \n<p>If you want to level-up a bit, you might want to write some scripts. The idea is to have three directories for each demo: Begin, Complete, and Initial. The idea is that your script copies your initial state directories over your presentation directory.</p> \n<h3>You Don't Have To Write Then All From Scratch</h3> \n<p>You don't necessarily have to write all of your own demos. More precisely, there's no perceived value to your students / attendees in presenting novel, amazing demos that don't actually accomplish something. Your demos should be optimized for the time and opportunity. Lately I've been building some simple code by hand, then showing a completed example someone else has published. I'll dig in to the code and show them how the exact concepts and code we used in the simple Hello World example work in a completed application.</p> \n<p>Ask around - Twitter, social networks, co-workers, etc. Often someone has a great demo already written, waiting for you to show it off. That lets you focus on the presentation, not building a new demo from scratch.</p> \n<h2>Presentation</h2> \n<h3>Managing expectations</h3> \n<p>Be really clear with expectations and roadmap throughout the day.</p> \n<ol> \n <li>Show the outline at the start of the day and set expectations on what you will and won’t be covering. </li> \n <li>Refer back to the outline regularly during the day – it doesn’t hurt to have a “you are here” at the start of each talk. It also cuts down on questions about things that will be covered in a later session. </li> \n <li>Wrap up at the end – helps people digest what they’ve learned (as explained earlier). </li> \n</ol> \n<p>Things make a lot more sense if you're providing the conceptual framework for attendees to file the content away.</p> \n<h3>Slides</h3> \n<p>There are different philosophies on using slides in presentations. For training, my opinion is that they can really help, and it's best to keep them pretty standard.</p> \n<p>Here's what I think the slides should do:</p> \n<ol> \n <li>Make it easy to follow what you're talking about by showing structure, showing frequent \"you are here\" signposts, and giving plenty of context. </li> \n <li>Gives something for attendees to write down (or take pictures of). </li> \n <li>Give the speaker some cues. It's hard to remember where every single demo goes in a full day of training. Some Demo slides that just show the title of the feature or concept can cue you in so you're not wondering what's next or forgetting demos. </li> \n</ol> \n<p>The standard things apply - don't read slide after slide, don't make eye charts with tons of bullet points, etc. Use diagrams and pictures where possible. Use the slides to enhance the presentation, not as a crutch.</p> \n<h2>Rehearsal</h2> \n<p>It's time consuming, but it's important to take the time for a full rehearsal. If you're co-presenting, take turns presenting your sessions for the other speakers (screen sharing if you're remote). I know this is a big time investment and you won't want to do it - or you'll want to race through it - but this will really pay off.</p> \n<p>Two big things to pay attention to:</p> \n<ol> \n <li><strong>Timing</strong> - You know how it's tricky to nail timing a one hour talk? Multiply that by eight. You need to know which sessions are running long, which ones can be sped up if you're running late, etc. </li> \n <li><strong>Demo Transitions</strong> - You'll want to make sure you're able to transition smoothly between slides and demos, that the demos actually work when you do them consecutively, etc. </li> \n</ol> \n<h2>Cheat Sheet</h2> \n<p>As you're rehearsing, make notes on a one-page cheat sheet. Mine usually include reminders about important bits of code or steps for the demos, anything tricky about the demo reset steps or setup, and important points I miss in practicing. I keep mine with me when I speak, and the fact that I know it's sitting next to me means I rarely really need it. When you do need it, though, it saves your audience from watching you fumble around. And it's handy to glance at during travel, as well.</p> \n<h2>Tech Check</h2> \n<p>It's a good idea to get in to the place you'll be presenting early to make sure that your laptop works and check the resolution and font size are visible in the back seats. Attendees shouldn't have to suffer through any of that. If you absolutely can't schedule it, at least find out the tech specifics and make sure you've got whatever video adapters you'll need.</p> \n<h2>It's All About Preparation</h2> \n<p>You'll notice that pretty much all of the above covers how to prepare for the event itself. There are a few other small tips for during the event (e.g. have some energy bars or Five Hour Energy handy, ask questions at the beginning to verify attendee experience level), but if you've prepared correctly then most of the actual presentation is already done. <a href=\"http://seriouspony.com/blog/2013/10/4/presentation-skills-considered-harmful\">Just be the user interface for the content you've prepared. Disappear.</a></p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\"> \n <a href=\"http://feeds.feedburner.com/~ff/jongalloway?a=YtT53hCF6Dk:1vG3zscFthc:yIl2AUoC8zA\"><img C#","link":"https://weblogs.asp.net:443/jongalloway/new-book-pro-windows-8-1-development-with-xaml-and-c","description":"<a href=\"http://www.apress.com/9781430240471\"><img style=\"float: right;\" src=\"http://www.apress.com/media/catalog/product/cache/9/image/9df78eab33525d08d6e5fb8d27136e95/A/9/A9781430240471-3d_1.png\" /></a> \n<p>Between releases of my other book (<a href=\"http://www.wrox.com/WileyCDA/WroxTitle/Professional-ASP-NET-MVC-5.productCd-1118794753.html\">Professional ASP.NET MVC</a>, next release due out soon!) <a href=\"http://jesseliberty.com/2014/06/03/pro-windows-8-1-development-wxaml-and-c/\">Jesse Liberty</a> somehow talked me into collaborating with him on writing a book on Windows 8.1 development. Actually, it wasn't a hard sell, because I've <a href=\"http://www.bing.com/search?q=xaml+site%3Aweblogs.asp.net%2Fjongalloway\">done a good amount of XAML dev</a>, love C#, and Windows 8 / 8.1 development is a lot of fun. We co-wrote the table of contents, I wrote code samples and very rough drafts (sometimes just bullet points), and Jesse turned it into prose. That worked pretty well, but was a little too slow - especially once my work on Professional ASP.NET MVC 5 ramped up. Fortunately, <a href=\"https://twitter.com/skimedic\">Phil Japiske</a> agreed to join us and finish the book off. Phil really nailed it - he polished off the remaining chapters, reviewed and greatly improved the existing chapters, and made sure everything was up to date for Windows 8.1.</p> \n<p><a href=\"http://www.apress.com/9781430240471\">Pro Windows 8.1 Development with XAML and C#</a> is focused on teaching you how to use your C# skills to build Windows Store applications. It teaches you the necessary XAML skills, the controls, the application model, and takes you from File / New to publishing an application to the Windows Store.</p> \n<p>Here's the table of contents:</p> \n<ol> \n <li>Getting Started <li>Building Your First Windows 8 App <li>Themes, Panels, and Controls <li>Binding <li>Views <li>Local Data <li>Remote Data and Services <li>Search and Share Contracts <li>Notifications <li>Application Life Cycle <li>Making Money <li>Publishing Your App </li></li></li></li></li></li></li></li></li></li></li></li>\n</ol> \n<p>I'm especially happy with my contributions to the local and remote data chapters. Any non-trivial application needs to tackle these concerns, and it's important to get right. I went through a few iterations on the pattern for data access and settled on a Task based repository pattern that worked pretty well. For the remote data, I showed how to set up Web API services both as JSON/XML endpoints and leveraging OData.</p> \n<p>I also wrote a lot of the Notifications chapter, since the primary example demonstrates using Azure Mobile Services to send client notifications.</p>\n<p>The book is a little over 300 pages, so it hopefully strikes a pretty good balance on the thorough vs. longwinded scale. I hope you like it!</p> \n<p><strong>Today (Friday June 13, 2014) there's a 40% discount code on the Apress site: FRDY13.</strong></p>\n<div class=\"feedflare\"> \n <a 09:30:59","updatedTime":"2020-08-25 09:30:59","title":"weblogs.asp.net Now Running On Orchard and Azure Web Sites","link":"https://weblogs.asp.net:443/jongalloway/weblogs-asp-net-now-running-on-orchard-and-azure-web-sites","description":"<p>I'm incredibly happy to announce that my blog - and all 750 other blogs running under weblogs.asp.net - are now powered by Orchard and Azure Web Sites! That's 750 blogs, all hosted in 4 Azure Web Sites all running on only one large virtual machine.</p> \n<h2>A bit of nostalgia</h2> \n<p style=\"float: right; padding-left: 10px\"><a title=\"Olde Tyme Blogge\" href=\"https://web.archive.org/web/20031018232745///weblogs.asp.net/jgalloway/posts/7498.aspx\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"Olde Tyme Blogge\" src=\"https://farm6.static.flickr.com/5077/14205986930_ed4cc80451.jpg\" /></a></p> \n<p>I've been blogging on weblogs.asp.net since May 2003 - over eleven years. Back then it was called <a href=\"https://web.archive.org/web/20030208002715///dotnetweblogs.com/\">dotnetweblogs</a> and ran on an open source blogging platform called .Text. Over the years, it got moved through varivous versions of Community Server, and had been stuck on an old, customized version of Community Server for almost seven years. It was hard to take myself seriously when blogging about web standards when my blog was being forced to quirks mode in IE due to an ancient HTML header.</p> \n<p>I'd exported my BlogML several times and thought about moving, but I really didn't want to. I'd been part of the weblogs.asp.net community for a long time - for a while I subscribed to every single RSS feed and read each of them daily - and I'm a pretty big ASP.NET fan, too. I resorted to cramming a bunch of JavaScript into the \"News and Announcements\" HTML field on the left. It pulled in jQuery from a CDN and did all kinds of terrible things to insert widgets and hide elements I didn't want and make the page jump around for a few seconds while loading. Kind of like most news websites these days, so maybe I was ahead of my time.</p> \n<p>I'd campaigned internally a few times to get the blog platform updated, but understood it was a big job to update several hundred weblogs - with history, and without downtime. Fortunately <a href=\"https://weblogs.asp.net/scottgu\">there's another very high profile blogger on the weblogs.asp.net platform</a>; that may have had something to do with getting the update to happen. :-)</p> \n<p>So I was extremely happy to hear that there was finally an effort underway to make the move!</p> \n<p><em>Disclaimer: I did not do any of the dev work for this migration. In fact, I was one of the top whiny customers - my blog was one of the test cases for the migration, and I gave <a href=\"https://twitter.com/sebastienros\">Sebastien Ros</a> and the Neudesic dev team (<a href=\"https://www.linkedin.com/in/pfuerte\">Peter Fuerte</a>, Samir Patel, </em><em><a href=\"https://www.linkedin.com/in/zachall\">Zac Hall</a>, </em><em>and <a href=\"https://twitter.com/terrimorton\">Terri Morton</a>)</em><em> tons of feedback and piles of feature requests.</em> <h2>Hosting Details</h2> <p>Orchard is hosted as <b>Azure Websites</b> on a single basic large instance using using 4 websites on a single instance to scale out the memory limit on 32 bits.</p> <p>We use <strong>4 Azure SQL Database</strong> instances, Business edition set to 10GB. Each azure website points to a SQL Azure database, and each database contains around 200 blog using table prefixes.</p> <p>Each website points to an <b>Azure Cache Service</b> for output caching</p> <p>All blogs point to the same <b>blob storage </b>account</p> <p>An A<b>zure Queue</b> is used to synchronize the main portal and the blogs</p> <p>One ARR proxy in an <b>Azure VM</b> is used to route the requests to the Azure websites. We set it up this way because we already had this VM set up to do the proxy work for the rest of *.asp.net.</p> <p>Here's a general diagram (stolen with permission from Sebastien's talk at the Orchard Harvest conference this week):</p> <p><a title=\"weblogs.asp.net infrastructure\" href=\"https://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/14393514942/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"weblogs.asp.net infrastructure\" src=\"https://farm4.static.flickr.com/3903/14393514942_95c546e130_b.jpg\" /></a></p> <h2>Performance Statistics</h2> <p>Here are our monthly estimated statistics for weblogs.asp.net:</p> \n <ul> \n  <li>Bandwidth out – 17 terrabytes <li>Page Views – 1.3 million <li>Unique Visitors – 750,000 <li>Visits – 1.1 million </li></li></li></li> \n </ul> <p>Here's the thing: Azure Web Sites is fast and efficient. I was already convinced of that, having run a lot of sites on it over the past few years. But <strong>Orchard is crazy efficient</strong>, too. I did not know that. I recently saw Sebastien give a presentation on Orchard, and it blew my mind. If you had heard or read that Orchard is slow, bloated or inefficient, you owe it to yourself to look again. I've been shocked by how fast and efficient the current release of Orchard is.</p> <p>The Orchard output cache module includes pretty moderate caching but delivers pretty extreme CPU performance benefits, as shown in the CPU charts from the production site below. Notice we're hovering around 20% CPU most of the time:</p> <p><a title=\"weblogs.asp.net output caching\" href=\"https://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/14415042433/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"weblogs.asp.net output caching\" src=\"https://farm4.static.flickr.com/3911/14415042433_bf8ff11349_b.jpg\" /></a></p> <h2>Hosting Cost Summary</h2> <p>It's incredibly cheap to host all 750 weblogs on Azure Web Sites, SQL Database, Storage and Cache. Here's a per-month cost estimate (keep the above stats in mind when you read these):</p> \n <table class=\"table table-striped table-condensed table-hover\"> \n  <tbody> \n   <tr> \n    <td>Item </td> \n    <td>Usage to Date </td> \n    <td>Cost to Date </td> \n    <td>Estimated Cost Per Month </td> \n   </tr> \n   <tr> \n    <td> <p>Data Transfer OUT (GB) - Zone 1</p></td> \n    <td> <p>1428.41 (GB)</p></td> \n    <td> <p>170.81</p></td> \n    <td>196.43 </td> \n   </tr> \n   <tr> \n    <td> <p>Data Transfer IN (GB) - Zone 1</p></td> \n    <td> <p>1.21 (GB)</p></td> \n    <td> <p>0.00</p></td> \n    <td>0.00 </td> \n   </tr> \n   <tr> \n    <td> <p>Database Units (in 10s) - Business Edition</p></td> \n    <td> <p>0.54 DB Units (10s)</p></td> \n    <td> <p>53.88</p></td> \n    <td>61.96 </td> \n   </tr> \n   <tr> \n    <td> <p>Storage Transactions (in 10,000s) - Data Management</p></td> \n    <td> <p>323.84 (10,000s)</p></td> \n    <td> <p>0.16</p></td> \n    <td>0.18 </td> \n   </tr> \n   <tr> \n    <td> <p>Basic (128 MB) - Cache</p></td> \n    <td> <p>2.52 (128 MB)</p></td> \n    <td> <p>31.52</p></td> \n    <td>50.00 </td> \n   </tr> \n   <tr> \n    <td> <p>Data Transfer (GB) - Zone 1</p></td> \n    <td> <p>0.08 (GB)</p></td> \n    <td> <p>0.01</p></td> \n    <td>0.01 </td> \n   </tr> \n   <tr> \n    <td> <p>Standard IO - Block Blob (GB) - Geo Redundant</p></td> \n    <td> <p>1.92 (GB)</p></td> \n    <td> <p>0.09</p></td> \n    <td>0.10 </td> \n   </tr> \n   <tr> \n    <td> <p>Large Basic Web Sites Hours - Web Sites</p></td> \n    <td> <p>609.00 (hours)</p></td> \n    <td> <p>187.20</p></td> \n    <td>215.28 </td> \n   </tr> \n   <tr> \n    <td>Grand Total </td> \n    <td>&nbsp;</td> \n    <td> <p>443.67</p></td> \n    <td>523.47 </td> \n   </tr> \n  </tbody> \n </table> <p>This works out to under about 70 cents per blog per month, which is pretty impressive.</p> <h2>What was changed in Orchard to support multi-blog</h2> <p>Nothing!</p> <p><a href=\"https://docs.orchardproject.net/Documentation/Setting-up-a-multi-tenant-orchard-site\">Multi-tenancy is a native capability of Orchard CMS</a>. It is the primary reason we chose Orchard as the platform. So that's neat.</p> <p>We're using 4 websites on a single Azure instance to scale out the memory limit on 32 bits, and any optimizations that were made to Orchard itself for this project were rolled back into the core platform (for v.1.8.1.0 and later).</p> <h2>Migrating 750 weblogs (with more than 75k posts and 250k comments)</h2> <p>weblogs.asp.net supported <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/BlogML\">BlogML</a> export and import. BlogML is an open format for Blog content, and it includes everything you'd need to migrate eleven years of blogging - including posts, comments and tags. It's not a slam dunk, though. Years ago, I tried to export my blog and the requests kept timing out because the export (including comment spam) was something like 5 or 6 MB. A year ago I was sick with a flu so I decided to do some blog spam analysis; <a href=\"https://twitter.com/terrimorton\">Terri</a> and team incorporated the results into their spam filters which cut the spam levels quite significantly. Still, lots of data.</p> <p>On the Orchard side, they took advantage of <a href=\"https://docs.orchardproject.net/Documentation/Making-a-Web-Site-Recipe\">custom Orchard recipes</a> to handle the import. Recipes are great - they include everything you'd need to initialize a new site, including blogs, users, etc. And they can be based off a template, so you can create multiple (say, 750) recipes from one template. Slick.</p> <p>At a high level (because I didn't write the code), Sebastien and friends tackled the problem like this:</p> \n <ul> \n  <li>Export all sites to BlogML <li>Convert the BlogML to custom Orchard recipes to create the new blogs including: \n    <ul> \n     <li>Posts <li>Comments <li>Users <li>New passwords </li></li></li></li> \n    </ul> <li>Generate custom commands to automate the recipe processing </li></li></li> \n </ul> <p>Automation was important, because they reran this migration several times.</p> <p>One other neat thing about the import: they used the VM Berserker technique (my name, I hope it sticks). You may have read about this approach before on Troy Hunt's post titled <a href=\"https://www.troyhunt.com/2013/12/working-with-154-million-records-on.html\">Working with 154 million records on Azure Table Storage – the story of “Have I been pwned?”</a></p> <p>The VM Berserker technique takes advantages of one of the most unappreciated values of cloud computing: the ability to develop cheaply, scale way up for a short time, and then turn off. This lets you do big things for less than the cost of a cup of coffee. Scaling up is nice. Scaling up and then back down is even nicer. So for this migration, they tested things out on a VM, then scaled it up to handle the migration, then turned it off (to stop paying). Done. Oh, and by running in an Azure VM they were running the processing in the same datacenter as the data, so it was really fast.</p> <p><strong>This is the kind of thing that tells me that cloud computing is the future. Sure, it's great to spin up sites and VM's and worker roles and stuff in the cloud. It's great that I can amp them up when I want. But often lost in the discussion is the power of being able to scale hard for an hour or two, then shut down. The cost-per-awesome on that transaction hasn't sunk in with most developers yet.</strong></p> <p>Sebastien announced at the Orchard Harvest conference this week that the tools that were written to migrate the content from the old system to Orchard (running Orchard recipes against BlogML export files) will be released publicly very soon.</p> <h2>New Blog Goodness</h2> <p>So much good stuff!!!</p> <h3>New Bootstrap blog theme</h3> <p>This is probably my favorite.&nbsp; In the past I'd written a few custom or inline styles, but now the system's running on a great framework. Bootstrap's tons of fun. There are individual styles and components that make things look better, so I can throw in a Bootstrap table style or grid whenever it's useful.</p> <p><button class=\"btn btn-default\"><span class=\"glyphicon glyphicon-star\"></span>This is a button with a star on it.</button></p> <p>Plus, it's easy to drop in a new theme from time to time. I <a href=\"https://bootswatch.com/\">grabbed one off Bootswatch</a> to get started, but am looking forward to tinkering with it.</p> <h3>Mobile friendly display</h3> <p>Since Bootstrap 3 is a mobile first framework, it always looks good on smaller displays. Posts automatically scale down for mobile devices using media queries.</p> <h3>More Authoring Options</h3> <p>I've always been a fan of Live Writer, and that's still supported. And there's HTML support, which is good. But Orchard also introduces Markdown editing support, which I'm told will cause my pants to shrink and my beard to grow out. So that's something.</p> <h3>Widgets!</h3> <p style=\"float: right; padding-bottom: 10px; padding-top: 10px; padding-left: 10px; padding-right: 10px\"><a title=\"Orchard Widget Zones\" href=\"https://www.flickr.com/photos/36836555@N00/14371595856/\"><img border=\"0\" alt=\"Orchard Widget Zones\" src=\"https://farm4.static.flickr.com/3856/14371595856_2f32e0f822.jpg\" /></a></p> <p>I'd been sneakily creating widgets for years by injecting HTML and JavaScript, but now there's real support. Orchard has some nice widgets, and you can drop them into 19 different zones, as shown on the right.</p> <p>As I was beta testing, I asked Sebastien if we could move the comments from the content to an after content module so I could insert custom HTML widgets above the comments, and he set that up.&nbsp; So far, the widgets have been incredibly flexible. No complaints at all there.</p> <h3>Spam Control</h3> <p>As I mentioned earlier, the spam was a bit out of hand before. Orchard adds <a href=\"https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ReCAPTCHA\">ReCAPTCHA</a> support. No messing around now, I can barely pass it.</p> <h3>Social Media Tag support</h3> <p>Orchard automatically includes support for all the fancy og:tags that are so hot these days. <a href=\"https://www.sebastienros.com/adding-social-meta-tags-in-orchard\">Sebastien wrote a post with more information</a>.</p> <h3>Custom Script Support</h3> <p>I have already admitted to jamming script tags in HTML areas on the previous site. The new site includes the ability to add custom scripts for analytics, syntax highlighting, etc. It's supported, and I can include them in the header or footer as appropriate.</p> <p>Site Nav Integration</p> <p>Now new pages can be integrated into the site navigation. Cool.</p> <h2>More Goodness!</h2> <p>There's a lot more! If you're running a blog that's hosted on weblogs.asp.net, see the updated support <a href=\"https://www.asp.net/community/support/weblogs\">here</a>. If you'd like to learn more about Orchard - or contribute, as it's an open source project - check out the <a href=\"https://orchardproject.net/\">Orchard Project</a>. I'll be posting more about some things I've learned in customizing my blog a bit, including the code formatting. </p> \n <div class=\"feedflare\"> \n  <a
